# Strategy Notes 

## General 

This is a workshop file -- yep, that means ontologically not perfect!
And, as we debate with this at the end of this week, we will DEFINITELY
add cards if needed when we note holes. This gives you a good sense of
the general approach you can you take on the aff and the neg as you move
into the year!

## Aff

The plan options all viable -- and you should think about what might be
the most strategic. The first plan text option does the most to explain
what the Lezak and Rock evidence says is necessary to address capacity
gap in accessing development resources.

T defense -- in addition the CI ev, most of our evidence talks about
development and infrastructure development in the Arctic and Alaska; the
cards are good about why the indigenous perspective on development is
necessary to challenge colonialism.

Climate only version just jettisons the framing -- and adds some cards
to the advantage. I have put a \* next to the cards that are probably
pretty important -- if it's too long, then you could remove the non \*
cards. I would suggest this if you don't think you will get much out of
the framing contention. The framing contention, however has a lot of
good stuff against Das as well Ks. The climate with framing version has
a lot of offense in it -- including discussions of indigenous
sovereignty

Several grant programs have been cut by Trump -- and yes we would defend
that the programs are restored \[and that's the language in plans like
including restoration of development grant programs.

Ask if you have questions!

## Neg 

Cap, set col, and security \[with climate links\] kinda best options --
while this may not be how you roll at home \[reading Ks\], this will be
a good chance to try out some stuff! Set col link debate focuses on the
role of reforming the states -- and while there's a lot of aff offense,
this will be a good link versus link turn/perm debates. We may put out a
supplement if a DA about energy development being bad comes together.
There's some offense on the case about climate catastrophizing. T --
development and T- Arctic \[that includes xtra T arg for practice\].

Ask if you have questions!

# 1AC 

## Plan options 

#### Plan: The United States federal government should initiate federal-Tribal collaborations regarding development through infrastructural planning; hire Tribal liaisons to coordinate with Alaskan Native communities for grant program evaluation; and develop joint federal-Tribal agendas in the Arctic, including restoration of development grant programs. 

#### Plan -- The United States federal government should significantly increase its exploration and/or development of the Arctic by increasing collaboration with indigenous groups in US territories in the Arctic.

#### Plan -- The United States federal government should significantly increase its exploration and/or development of the Arctic by expanding federal-tribal collaborations regarding development of the Arctic. 

## 1ac -- climate with framing 

### Climate Advantage

#### Trump's recent bill eliminated most programs that supported green transition and adaptation programs for indigenous groups

Loan Guarantee Program, or [[TELGP]{.mark}.]{.underline} Initially

[Those cuts]{.underline}]{.mark} [could further jeopardize ongoing

awaiting a decision from the U.S. Court of Appeals.

[The [Arctic region plays a pivotal role in the global climate system

is a [key]{.mark} [driver of Arctic amplification]{.mark}, where the
region warms at a faster rate than the global average]{.underline}. As

the Northern Hemisphere. A positive phase of the AO is associated with a

while a negative phase can lead to colder and more severe winters.

#### And, these same climate realities mean that squo approaches to Arctic development focus on the "Arctic resource boom" imposing a laundry list of environmental impacts on Indigenous groups in the Arctic that replicates colonial power structures characteristic of engagement of the region over time 

**Moeser**, graduate of the Master of Science in Development Practice
(MDP) program at Trinity College Dublin, **2023**

\[Elizabeth, "Decolonization and Arctic Engagement: A Critical Analysis
of Resource Development in the US Arctic," The Arctic Institute, Feb 21,
2023,
https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/decolonization-arctic-engagement-critical-analysis-resource-development-us-arctic/,
HDSCW 07-12-2025 IS\]

An estimated [10 percent of the nearly four million residents throughout
the entire Arctic region are Indigenous]{.underline}.1) Indigenous
populations have inhabited much of the Arctic for thousands of years,
long before the land was claimed and divided into nation-states.
Interest in opportunities for natural resource extraction drove the
historic colonization in the Arctic2) and, [today, the
increasing]{.underline} [accessibility of minerals and oil due to
climate change is fuelling the]{.underline} so-called "**[Arctic
resource boom]{.underline}**"3)---[a quest to]{.underline} find and
**[exploit]{.underline}** [the region's remaining
resources.]{.underline} [As more international players hope to
participate in Arctic affairs, it is **critical** to not only consider
how these ventures impact **Indigenous populations** but to prioritize
**decolonization** in these spaces]{.underline}.

[Climate change is occurring more rapidly in the Arctic]{.underline}
than in any other part of the world, with the most recent evidence
pointing to a rate of warming nearly four times greater than the global
average.4) This [warming is driving the thawing of permafrost, loss of
seasonal snow cover, and melting of sea ice, ice caps and glaciers;
each,]{.underline} in turn, [increasing the **accessibility** of
**natural resources** and growing **global interest** in the
Arctic]{.underline}.5) This article uses a decolonization framework to
analyze resource development with a focus on the United States (US),
which is considered an Arctic state by way of Alaska. [Decolonization
will be defined as a concept and practice of confronting and reversing
the historic and ongoing legacies of colonialism,]{.underline}6)
[including the structures of power that uphold
inequalities]{.underline}.

After a brief background covering land ownership and some of the
outcomes of resource development in Alaska, this article will delve into
dynamics that reproduce and perpetuate colonial structures of power.
This analysis will feature (i) the prioritization of state and federal
interests through the idea of sovereignty and (ii) the primacy and
assumed superiority of Western knowledge and conventions and,
conversely, the disregard for Indigenous knowledge systems and
worldviews. To bridge the gap between theory and action, the article
will continually ask what it would mean to decolonize resource
development in the US Arctic. [**Confronting** the ongoing systems and
dynamics of **colonialism** must be placed at the **forefront** of
present-day and future Arctic engagement and
collaboration]{.underline}---not only in the US, but [throughout the
region]{.underline}

Background

The United States purchased the land that is present-day Alaska from
Russia in 1867. Just over a century later, in 1968, the discovery of oil
in Prudhoe Bay made the Arctic relevant at a national scale.7) The
commercial export of oil to the lower 48 states was now possible for the
first time. Hindering this ambition, however, was the fact that the
planned pipeline to carry the oil south traversed lands claimed by
Alaska Native populations. 'Alaska Native' refers collectively to the
Indigenous populations who have lived in present-day Alaska for
thousands of years before the arrival of Europeans in the 1700s.8) This
term encompasses "many nations and tribes" and "widely diverse cultures,
languages, lifeways, art forms and histories".9) To enable the
construction of the pipeline to the contiguous United States, land
claims issues around Prudhoe Bay were settled with the Alaska Native
Claims Settlement Act (ANCSA) in 1971. ANCSA extinguished Indigenous
claims to nearly 400 million acres of land throughout the state in
exchange for \$962.5 million and ownership of 43.7 million acres.

ANCSA also divided the state into twelve regions, each with an Alaska
Native Regional Corporation. The regional boundaries of the corporations
do not, however, equate to land ownership; land in Alaska is
predominantly owned by the state and federal governments. Just 10
percent of Alaska's 663,000 square miles are owned by Alaska Native
populations.10) The Regional Corporations operate under US corporate law
to administer resources and serve the local communities. These
for-profit corporations participate in the Alaskan natural resource
industry, both directly and indirectly.

[Federal Arctic policy in the US]{.underline} [has tended to
emphasize]{.underline} the [exploitation]{.underline} [of Arctic
resources for US economic development]{.underline}.11) W[ithin the new
National Strategy for the Arctic Region]{.underline} (released October
2022), [economic development continues to receive a strong
emphasis---alongside security---and Arctic investments are expected to
increase over the following decade]{.underline}.12) In the face of this
ever-growing interest in Arctic engagement, [Alaska Native
communities]{.underline} may [face new and renewed **colonial
intrusion** in the form of **large-scale resource
development**]{.underline}.13)

Effects of resource development

Continual '[**boom and bust**']{.underline} [with periods of financial
growth and decline characterizes resource development in the
**capitalist system**]{.underline}.14) This [instability]{.underline}
[prohibits]{.underline} many [communities from relying]{.underline}
solely [on economies centered on resource extraction]{.underline}.
[Subsistence practices provide a source of greater
stability]{.underline} within what would otherwise be an unstable
extractive economy.15) [**Resource extraction** has]{.underline},
however, [had **detrimental impacts** on the land, water, and wildlife
that reduce the viability of subsistence lifeways.]{.underline}
Specifically, [extractive activities have been found to **increase**
**vulnerability** to **biological invasion**]{.underline},16) [bring
changes to the land with built infrastructure]{.underline},17) [and
introduce the risk of contamination, such as **oil
spills**]{.underline}.18) [The resulting decreased viability of
subsistence practices is worsened by the threat climate change poses to
hunting and gathering activities]{.underline}.19)

Although some measures of physical health have improved since ANCSA,20)
[resource extraction can compromise the **health** and **wellbeing** of
nearby Indigenous populations.]{.underline} [Significant health
disparities remain among Alaska Natives compared to
non-native]{.underline} Alaskans and other US [residents]{.underline}.
Generally, [Alaska Natives have a **lower life expectancy**, increased
risk of **cardiovascular disease**, and an **elevated burden of
infectious disease**.]{.underline}21) [By exacerbating **food
insecurity**, the negative impacts of resource development may
contribute to and **worsen health outcomes**.]{.underline}
[Many]{.underline} of these disparities [are rooted in]{.underline} the
history of [colonialism]{.underline} [and]{.underline} [socio-political
marginalization]{.underline}; in addition, [their persistence today may
be attributed to the disproportionate exposure to environmental health
hazards]{.underline}.22) Moreover, some researchers have found that
[health data fails to produce a holistic picture of well-being,
suggesting that there must be a greater consideration for
context-specific understandings of health status]{.underline}.23)

Importantly, [the effects of resource development are not felt uniformly
by Alaska Natives]{.underline}. Instead, [various facets of identity
among individuals and communities impact how they relate to extractive
activities]{.underline}. [Socioeconomic class divisions]{.underline},24)
[gender identity]{.underline},25) [one's location in a rural versus
urban setting]{.underline},26) [and many other factors can all impact
how resource development is experienced]{.underline}. [The
**complexity** of the relationship]{.underline} between resource
development and Indigenous populations, and the diversity within the
Alaska Native population, [**must not be ignored** or
simplified]{.underline}.

#### In fact, current development and resource extraction ignores Indigenous governance systems that advocate for sustainable resource management -- only the aff solves

**Malik,** School of Geography, University of Leeds UK, **and Ford,**
Priestley Centre for Climate Futures UK, **25**

\[Ishfaq Hussain, James D, "Barriers and limits to adaptation in the

[Many national [governments prioritise **neoliberal development** and
**resource extraction**]{.mark}]{.underline} (mining, oil, and gas),
[[while **Indigenous governance** systems advocate for **sustainable
resource management**]{.underline}]{.mark} \[103,••32,92\]. In Norway,
the government's focus on oil and gas development often conflicts with
Indigenous demands for sustainable re- source management \[33\]. [[This
prioritisation of resource extraction over environmental and cultural
preservation acts as a barrier to adaptation]{.underline}]{.mark}. In
Russia, large-scale oil and gas projects --- such as those in the Yamal
Peninsula --- have led to forced relocations of Nenets and Evenki
reindeer herders, significantly reducing their ability to maintain
traditional migration routes \[107,44\]. [In Alaska and Canada,
governments continue to grant resource ex- traction permits on
Indigenous lands, despite widespread resistance from Indigenous
communities]{.underline} \[28,5\]. The oil drilling Willow Project in
Alaska, approved in 2023, has faced intense opposition from Indigenous
activists who argue that it will increase environmental destruction,
disrupt wildlife migration patterns, and accelerate per- mafrost thawing
\[31\]. However, the project was still ap- proved, highlighting how
governance systems frequently privilege economic interests over
Indigenous sovereignty and long-term climate resilience.

[[**Indigenous communities** are often **marginalised** in **gov-
ernance**, **decision-making processes**, **adaptation plan- ning**, and
**consultation** in resource extraction and face **challenges** in
asserting their rights and interests]{.underline}]{.mark} \[103,97\].
[[This **exclusion** creates **significant barriers** to
adaptation]{.mark}, [as policies that do not incorporate Indigenous
perspec- tives often fail to address the realities of Arctic

Many of [[the **governance failures** in the Arctic are **rooted** in

and forced relocation, which have collec- tively impeded
and]{.underline}]{.mark}, in certain cases, [[severed the im- portant

Indigenous communities have permanently lost land, mobility, and

[[Colonialism and injustice have evolved into government policies
limiting access to resources]{.underline}]{.mark} such as small-scale
Indigenous Peoples' fisheries and favouring offshore traw- lers,

\[94\]. [Historical, interpersonal, epistemic, and systemic racism has

[This presents a limit and undermines the adaptive
capacity]{.underline}.

[[**Colonisation** has systematically **turned** the natural con-
straints in the Arctic into **severe socioeconomic barriers** by
**undermining** Indigenous **autonomy** and **severing traditional
adaptation strategies**]{.underline}]{.mark} \[••65\]. Forced se-
dentarisation, land dispossession, and restrictive re- source governance
have stripped Indigenous communities of their ability to flexibly
respond to en- vironmental challenges \[104\]. [[Colonial policies have
re- inforced]{.underline} the [infrastructural
limitations]{.underline}]{.mark} [--- [investing in resource extraction
rather than local development, leaving Indigenous communities with high
costs of living, food insecurity, and limited access to essential
service]{.mark}s]{.underline} \[55,90\]. [[What were once manageable
ecological limits have thus been compounded by imposed social,
political, and economic constraints, shaping the struc- tural
inequalities that define contemporary Arctic adap- tation
challenges]{.underline}.]{.mark}

[Colonialism has created both barriers and limits to adaptation by
forcibly relocating Indigenous commu- nities to environmentally
unsuitable and high-risk areas]{.underline}, where traditional hunting
and fishing practices are no longer viable. While initially a barrier,
this [displacement has evolved into a structural limit, as these
communities are now deeply established, making relocation im- practical
despite increasing climate risks]{.underline}. State-imposed wildlife
regulations restrict Indigenous adaptive flex- ibility, preventing
communities from adjusting their subsistence practices in response to
environmental changes \[22\]. These [[policies]{.underline}, often
[designed without Indigenous input]{.underline}, [reinforce colonial
constraints on adaptation, further limiting the agency of Arctic In-
digenous Peoples in navigating climate challenges]{.underline}]{.mark}.

The [[geopolitical competition]{.underline}]{.mark} among major powers
such as the United States, Russia, Canada, and China; the [[esca- lation
of military operations]{.underline} by Arctic nations; the ex[- pansion
of commercial shipping routes through the Arctic]{.underline}; [and the
exploration of oil, gas, and mineral resources]{.underline} [have
emerged as important limits affecting Arctic governance and
adaptation]{.underline} \[]{.mark}30,79\]. The geopolitics of the Arctic
has gained global attention due to climate change, resulting in
increased interest in commercial shipping and energy resources \[95\].
The reduction of sea ice has triggered a surge in human activities where
countries and corpora- tions vie for control over resources and shipping
routes, diverting attention and resources for adaptation. This is
exacerbated by increasing militarisation of the Arctic \[101\],
affecting environmental and human sustainability and security and
long-term adaptation planning. Russia has significantly expanded
military bases in the Arctic, while the U.S. and NATO allies have
increased defence spending \[57\]. This geopolitical tension reduces
interna- tional cooperation on climate adaptation, as nations prioritise
strategic control over adaptation funding and environmental protection.
Geopolitical competition is a structural limit, as it shifts governance
priorities away from climate resilience and human security and toward
national security concerns.

Conclusion

[[Addressing barriers and limits to climate change adap- tation requires
transformative structural changes that recognise Indigenous rights,
knowledge, and sover- eignty, and a move towards **inclusive
governance**]{.underline}]{.mark}. [[These changes can be achieved
through targeted policies and frameworks]{.underline},]{.mark} such as
the Arctic Council's Sustainable Development Working Group, [which
facilitates colla- boration on climate resilience strategies grounded in
Indigenous knowledge.]{.underline} [Initiatives like co-management
agreements between Indigenous communities and na- tional governments
provide effective models for fos- tering inclusive
decision-making.]{.underline} [[Investing in **resilient
infrastructure**, promoting **Indigenous knowledge sys- tems**, and
ensuring **social** and **economic equity** are im- portant steps toward
**enhancing adaptation capacity** and **long-term sustainability** in
the Arctic]{.underline}.]{.mark}

#### Current approaches to development create green sacrifice zones that ignore input of indigenous groups-- meh tag

In recent years[, the Arctic]{.underline} and sub-[Arctic regions have
seen an increased interest in investment in mining and low-carbon energy
infrastructure]{.underline}. As Fig. 2 shows, [at least 80 % of
low-carbon energy]{.underline} (all stages from planning to operating[)
and mineral]{.underline} (including exploration, construction,
operating, closure, and abandonment) [projects are intersected with

spoken and Indigenous peoples hold land based rights, interests, and
knowledges]{.underline}. [This]{.underline} number [could be even
higher, as the absence of Indigenous languages on the map does not
necessary mean there are no Indigenous peoples or land rights. And it is
likely that extractive and energy industries will expand]{.underline},
be it deep seabed mineral exploration, rare earths mining, wind energy

[Among multiple drivers behind this increase are energy security
concerns, global geopolitical re-alignments, and numerous policies and

(2020), the European Critical Raw Materials Act (2023), the US's
Inflation Reduction Act (2022), and Canada's Critical Minerals Strategy
(2023), among others[.]{.underline}

While these documents underscore the importance of balancing economic
development with social and environmental goals, [there is a need to
bring light to the distribution of burdens and opportunities of energy
transition in the circumpolar north. Central to this understanding is
that energy transitions in the circumpolar north are deeply intertwined
with the history of colonialism]{.underline}, as it continues to shape
the region's environmental and sociopolitical landscape[. Colonial
policies have been prioritizing extractive industries]{.underline} (such

region perceived as the "terra nullius", expropriating lands, and
marginalising Indigenous communities. Ongoing energy transitions are
intensifying resource extraction against the legacy of historical and
existing extractive industries and reproducing colonial attitudes as
decision-making continues to exclude Indigenous peoples]{.underline}.

[These dynamics have led to the framing of the region as a "green
sacrifice zone", providing a deep interrogation of neocolonial, racial,
cultural, and environmental underpinnings of making "green" industrial
economies. The concept of "sacrifice zones" has gained popularity in the
US regarding nuclear testing locations, waste sites and extractive
regions, which are "sacrificed" for a higher purpose in the name of
profit and progress, highlighting environments and populations that are
marginalised.]{.underline} The value of its application in the Arctic
context is that it "helps to see how the participation and distribution
of burdens and benefits are understood and considered" given the impacts
of industrial projects on communities driven by top-down policies and
the global demand for resources and low-carbon energy[. Issues of a just
energy transition in the Arctic have received increasing attention, with
the calls for evaluation of existing projects, legal instruments and
policy frameworks, as well as identification of gaps and areas for
improvement.]{.underline} This review addresses these needs.

#### Indigenous knowledge systems are critical to Arctic climate adaptation and limited by warming induced barriers BUT excluded from policy development. That reduces adaptive capacity and coping mechanisms.

Social norms

The inherent challenges of living in the Arctic constrain human
settlement, infrastructure, and economic activity. [Extreme climatic
conditions and short growing seasons render large-scale agriculture
unviable, necessitating reliance on subsistence practices or imported
food]{.underline} \[22,46\]. [**Sparse population** and]{.underline}
vast **[geographic expanses]{.underline}** significantly [**limit** the
**feasibility** of shared infrastructure]{.underline}, such as extensive
road networks and power grids, [in- creasing the **cost** and
**logistical complexity** of economic development]{.underline} \[11\].
[However, these]{.underline} same [conditions have historically provided
**ecological** and **cultural** **advantages for Indigenous
communities.**]{.underline} [Sparse human populations relative to
abundant fish and wildlife have sustained traditional subsistence
economies, ensuring food se- curity through hunting, fishing, and
herding]{.underline} \[23\]. [**The vast, open landscapes have
facilitated mobility, enabling seasonal resource access and fostering
resilient adapta- tion strategies**.]{.underline}

[[The **loss of traditional knowledge** is an **important barrier** to
**adaptation** in the Arctic.]{.underline} [External pressures such as
**globalisation** cause resource extraction, displacement, and
environmental degradation, impacting Indigenous knowledge systems,
cultural practices, languages, and institutions]{.underline} \[21\],
hence [perpetuating **epistemic injustice**]{.underline}]{.mark}.
[[Economic and environmental changes exacerbate this, causing
generational discontinuities in the transfer of traditional knowledge to
younger generations]{.underline} \[]{.mark}81\]. [[There is **limited
prioritisation** of Indigenous Peoples in adaptation policy development
and support to respond to climate impacts]{.underline}]{.mark}
\[10,13\]. [The [**lack of engagement** and **exclusion** of
**Indigenous Peoples**, **knowledge**, and **per- spectives** from
policy formulations]{.mark} [is a **barrier** that affects the
implementation of effective culturally sensitive adaptation strategies
and]{.mark} [policies tailored and relevant to Indigenous
Peoples]{.mark}]{.underline} \[49,87\].

[**Cultural practices** such as sharing networks, communal hunting, oral
traditions, storytelling, and connections to the land and ocean are
**affected** by loss of traditional hunting grounds and wildlife due to
**climate change impacts**]{.underline} \[29\], [affecting **food
security** and weakening **community agency**, **cultural identity**,
**resilience**, and **adaptive capacity**]{.underline}. [[Indigenous
knowledge systems are **important** for adaptation and are **at risk**
due to cultural assimilation, modernisation, generational gaps, and
transformative environmental changes]{.underline}]{.mark} \[108,77\].
[[This loss creates **barriers**]{.underline}, [as it **reduces adaptive
capacity** and **coping mechanisms**]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[undermining
the ability of com- munities to respond effectively to changing climatic
conditions.]{.underline} [It reduces sustainable resource
management]{.underline}]{.mark} [and adaptive capacities and practices
where [commu- nities could potentially lose critical insights that
inform locally appropriate and culturally sensitive adaptation
strategies]{.mark}]{.underline} \[23,87\].

In Scandinavia, [[Sami herding traditions]{.underline}, [which involve
adaptive rotational grazing to maintain reindeer popula-
tions]{.underline},]{.mark} [are]{.underline} often [disregarded in
conservation planning.]{.underline} [Instead, policymakers prioritise
European Union en- vironmental regulations that enforce fixed grazing
boundaries]{.underline} \[100,68\]. [In Canadian Arctic, younger gen-
erations are]{.underline} increasingly [disconnected from traditional
hunting practices due to the influence of modern life- styles, climate
change, and the decline in wildlife po- pulations]{.underline}
\[56,81\]. [[This **loss of traditional knowledge** creates a
**barrier**]{.mark} [to adaptation, as communities **lose the ability to
respond effectively to changing environmental
conditions**]{.mark}]{.underline}[.]{.mark}

The role of [gendered labour norms acts as a barrier and plays a
significant role in shaping adaptive capacity and
decision-making]{.underline}. [While men traditionally occupied roles as
hunters, herders, and fishermen, climate change is altering these
economic foundations, necessitating new employment
opportunities]{.underline} \[7\]. In Sami communities, men's ability to
continue reindeer herding is being un- dermined by shrinking pastures,
forcing women into new economic roles \[73\]. [In many Arctic
communities, women are leaving for education and employment op-
portunities, which can both mitigate and exacerbate
vulnerabilities]{.underline} \[12\]. While improved [education and job
access]{.underline} can enhance resilience, they [may]{.underline} also
[lead to the outmigration of skilled individuals, further straining
community cohesion and adaptive capacity]{.underline}. In Green- land,
many Indigenous women are leaving their communities to seek education
and employment in Denmark and urban areas, which provides economic
empowerment but also weakens intergenerational knowledge transfer and
community cohesion \[54,82\]. While these individual choices offer
economic mobility, they weaken the transmission of traditional
adaptation strategies, presenting both a barrier and a limit --- a limit
because the cultural shift away from subsistence prac- tices is often
irreversible.

[**Social norms** tied to traditional subsistence practices
are]{.underline} being [**fundamentally disrupted** by climate
change,]{.underline} [leading to limits on adaptation]{.underline}. [An
important example]{.underline} of this limit [is the loss of key
wildlife species essential to Indigenous diets and cultural
identity]{.underline}. The decline of caribou populations in the
Canadian Arctic has reached a point where some communities can no longer
rely on this culturally and nutritionally important species \[8,93\].
This represents a limit to adaptation, as the loss of car- ibou
undermines food security, cultural identity, and traditional practices,
significantly impacting Inuit com- munities. [[Conservation
efforts]{.underline}]{.mark}, such as restricting car- ibou hunting,
[[address ecological sustainability but force communities to abandon
traditional food-sharing net- works]{.underline}, [important for
survival and social cohesion]{.underline} \[42\]. In Gre]{.mark}enland,
Inuit hunters rely on sea ice for seal and whale hunting, which is
economically and culturally significant. However[, [with sea ice loss
accelerating, these traditional hunting grounds are
disappearing]{.underline} \[37\]. [This represents a limit --- without
ice, these practices cannot continue. This leads to both **food
insecurity** and **cultural erosion**]{.underline}]{.mark}.

#### The aff prioritizes decolonizing resource development to challenge cultural Europeanisation in development -- solves for environmental calamity

Prioritization of Western knowledge and conventions

[A **deep-rooted** **colonial mentality** that remains **pervasive** is
the assumed **supremacy** of a **Western knowledge
system**]{.underline}. [This creates a **paternalistic relationship**
wherein decisions are made by external 'experts' and imposed upon the
Indigenous population.]{.underline} In short, [Indigenous ways of
knowing are undermined.]{.underline}32) The term [Indigenous knowledge
is defined as "the understandings, skills and philosophies developed by
societies with long histories of interaction with their natural
surroundings".]{.underline}33) [[Efforts to decolonize resource
development in the US Arctic must not only embrace these different
worldviews, perspectives, and knowledge systems, but **put them
first**]{.underline}.]{.mark}

[There is]{.underline} also [[a tendency to assume that the only route
to]{.underline} achieving [greater material benefits]{.underline} or
power [is to adopt a capitalist
econom]{.underline}]{.mark}[y]{.underline}. What Aníbal Quijano---one of
the preeminent thinkers of decolonization theory---terms ['**[cultural
Europeanisation]{.underline}**]{.mark},' but can be understood as
colonial Western society more broadly, [[is taken as universal and
synonymous with being '**developed'**]{.mark}.]{.underline}34) Before
ANCSA was passed in 1971, Alaska Native populations had primarily
engaged in subsistence activities. Since the settlement of land claims
agreements, Alaska Native populations are increasingly entrenched in the
capitalist economy, especially via resource development. Today, most
Indigenous people in Alaska engage in a mixed economy, meaning a
combination of both subsistence and market-based activities.35[) [A
desire to obtain the benefits from natural resource extraction on
Indigenous lands is a primary reason for this growth in capitalist
engagement]{.underline}.36) [Through a decolonization lens, it is
understood that following the rhetoric and logic of Western society is a
way to "reach the same material benefits and the same power as the
Europeans: viz, to conquer nature in short for
'development'".]{.underline}37) [This model of
**development**]{.underline}, however, [has proven "**environmentally
calamitous on the global scale**",]{.underline}38) [made even more dire
by the rapid impact of climate change throughout the
Arctic]{.underline}.]{.mark}

### Framing 

#### Currently, resource extractions under development models deprioritizes indigenous perspectives, undermining indigenous sovereignty 

**Malik,** School of Geography, University of Leeds UK, **and Ford,**
Priestley Centre for Climate Futures UK, **25**

**\[Ishfaq Hussain Malik, and James D. Ford, "**Barriers and limits to
adaptation in the Arctic", [[Current Opinion in Environmental
Sustainability]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/journal/current-opinion-in-environmental-sustainability),
[[https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#ab0015]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#ab0015) 
April 2025, access date: 7/8/2025\] HDCSW LN 

[Many national **governments prioritise neoliberal development and
resource extraction** (mining, oil, and gas), **while Indigenous
governance systems advocate for sustainable resource management**
[103](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib103),
[32••](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib32),
[92](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib92).]{.underline}
In Norway, the government's focus on oil and gas development often
conflicts with Indigenous demands for sustainable resource management
[[\[33\]]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib33).
This prioritisation of resource extraction over environmental and
cultural preservation acts as a barrier to adaptation. In Russia,
large-scale oil and gas projects --- such as those in the Yamal
Peninsula --- have led to forced relocations of Nenets and Evenki
reindeer herders, significantly reducing their ability to maintain
traditional migration routes
[[107]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib107),
[[44](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib44).
**In Alaska**]{.underline} and Canada[**, governments continue to grant
resource extraction permits on Indigenous lands, despite widespread
resistance from Indigenous communities
[2](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib28)**[8](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib28),
[5](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib5).
The oil drilling Willow Project in Alaska, approved in
2023,]{.underline} has [faced intense opposition from Indigenous
activists who argue that it will increase environmental destruction,
disrupt wildlife migration patterns, and accelerate permafrost thawing
[\[31\]](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib31).
However, the project was still approved, highlighting how governance
systems frequently privilege economic interests over Indigenous
sovereignty and long-term climate resilience.]{.underline}

#### Squo resource development ensure that development is a colonial enterprise that privileges state sovereignty over indigenous perspectives. Only Indigenous sovereignty over resource development actualizes decolonization in the Arctic, addressing colonial violence 

**Moeser**, graduate of the Master of Science in Development Practice
(MDP) program at Trinity College Dublin, **2023**

\[Elizabeth, "Decolonization and Arctic Engagement: A Critical Analysis
of Resource Development in the US Arctic," The Arctic Institute, Feb 21,

Sovereignty and the prioritization of state interests

[To decolonize resource development]{.underline}, [Alaska Native
populations must have **greater control** over the process]{.underline}.
In other words, [one overarching aim of decolonization in practice is
**Indigenous sovereignty**.]{.underline} However, [**(neo)colonial
narratives** shape and are ingrained within the traditional concept of
**state-centered sovereignty**]{.underline}. Within this concept, [the
aims of the government are continually prioritized; a **hierarchy** is
established whereby the interests and concerns of the state are given
primacy]{.underline}.27) [This dynamic is]{.underline} one of the
reasons [why]{.underline}, in the case of Alaska and Alaska Native
populations, [resource development continues to be experienced as a
**colonial enterprise**]{.underline}. [The gains from extractive
activities do not necessarily or primarily benefit Alaska Native
populations]{.underline},28) [and the interests of outside actors are
prioritized]{.underline}.29) Oftentimes, [wealth]{.underline} from
resource development [has been extracted from northern regions to
benefit other populations]{.underline}.30) [An evaluation of the Red Dog
Mine]{.underline} in northwest Alaska, for instance, [found]{.underline}
[that the local population received a]{.underline} relatively [modest
share of benefits in terms of employment, income, and
mobility]{.underline}.31)

As established through ANCSA, the [Regional Corporations are the primary
route for Alaska Native participation in governing resource development
in the US Arctic. These corporations]{.underline}, however, [operate
under US corporate law and,]{.underline} therefore, [within the
**frameworks of the capitalist order**]{.underline}. Participation
through the structure of the [Regional Corporations]{.underline},
[thus]{.underline}, [limits the opportunity for Alaska Native
populations to achieve self-determination]{.underline} outside of those
conventions. In action, considering the embeddedness of the corporations
within the capitalist and colonial dynamics of the state,
[**decolonization** may necessitate **enhancing** the power of and
regard for **local governments** rather than governing resource
development principally through the Regional Corporations.]{.underline}

**Prioritize colonial violence in impact analysis -- extinction is an
empty superlative like 'all lives matter' and its appeals should be
rejected**

**Mitchell**, Professor and Canada Research Chair in Global Political
Ecology at Wilfrid Laurier University, **2017**

\[Audra, "Decolonizing against extinction part II: Extinction is not a
metaphor -- it is literally genocide," Worldly on Word Press, September
27, 2017,
https://worldlyir.wordpress.com/2017/09/27/decolonizing-against-extinction-part-ii-extinction-is-not-a-metaphor-it-is-literally-genocide/,
HDCSW - TM\]

Extinction is not a metaphor...

[[Extinction]{.mark} has become an [emblem]{.mark} of **Western**, and
[**white-dominated**, **fears**]{.mark} about 'the end of the(ir)
world'.]{.underline} [This scientific term is [saturated with emotional
potency]{.mark}]{.underline}, [stretched and contorted to embody almost
any **nightmare**]{.underline}, [from climate change to asteroid
strikes.]{.underline} In academic and public contexts alike, [it is
regularly interchanged with other terms and concepts -- for instance,
'species death', global warming or ecological collapse.]{.underline}
[Diffused into sublime scales]{.underline} -- mass extinctions measured
in millions of (Gregorian calendar) years, a planet totalized by the
threat of nuclear destruction -- ['[extinction' has become an **empty
superlative**]{.mark}]{.underline}, [one that that gestures to an
abstract form of unthinkability.]{.underline} [It teases Western
subjects with images of generalized demise]{.underline} [that
might]{.underline}, if it gets bad enough, even [threaten]{.underline}
us, or [the figure of 'humanity']{.underline} that we enshrine as a
universal. [[This figure of 'humanity']{.mark},]{.underline} derived
from Western European enlightenment ideals, [[emphasizes]{.mark}
individual, autonomous actors who are fully integrated into the global
market system;]{.underline} [who are [responsible citizens of
nation-states]{.mark}]{.underline}; who conform to Western ideas of
health and well-being; who partake of 'culture'; who participate in
democratic state-based politics; who refrain from physical violence; and
who manage their 'resources' responsibly (Mitchell 2014).

Oddly, **[[exposure to the fear of extinction contributes to the
formation and bolstering of contemporary Western
subjects]{.mark}.]{.underline}** [Contemplating the sublime destruction
of 'humanity' offers the thrill of abjection]{.underline}: the perverse
pleasure derived from exposure to something by which one is revolted.
Claire Colebrook detects this thrill-seeking impulse in the profusion of
Western blockbuster films and TV shows that imagine and envision the
destruction of earth, or at least of 'humanity'. It also throbs through
a flurry of recent best-selling books -- both fiction and speculative
non-fiction (see Oreskes and Conway 2014; Newitz 2013; Weisman 2008). In
a forthcoming intervention, Noah Theriault and I (2018) argue that
[[these imaginaries]{.mark} are a form of porn that [normalizes the
profound violences driving extinction,]{.mark} while cocooning its
viewers in the secure space of the voyeur.]{.underline} [Certainly,
there are many Western scientists, conservationists and policy-makers
who are genuinely committed to stopping the extinction of others,
perhaps out of fear for their own futures.]{.underline} [Yet
[**extinction is not quite real** for Western]{.mark}, and especially
white, [subjects]{.mark}]{.underline}[; [it is a **fantasy of negation**
that evokes **thrill**, **melancholy**, **anger** and **existential
purpose**]{.underline}]{.mark}[.]{.underline}

It is a metaphor that expresses the destructive desires of these beings,
and the negativity against which we define our subjectivity.

But **[[extinction is not a metaphor]{.underline}]{.mark}**: [[it is a
very real expression of violence that systematically destroys particular
beings, worlds, life forms and]{.mark} the [relations]{.mark} that
enable them to flourish.]{.underline} [These are real, unique beings,
worlds and relations]{.underline} -- as well as somebody's family,
Ancestors, siblings, future generations -- **[who are violently
destroyed.]{.underline}** [Extinction can only be used unironically as a
metaphor by people who have never been threatened with it, told it is
their inevitable fate, or lost their relatives and Ancestors to it --
and who assume that they probably never will.]{.underline}

This argument is directly inspired by the call to arms issued in 2012 by
Eve Tuck and Wayne K. Yang and more recently by Cutcha Risling-Baldy.
The first, seminal piece demonstrates how settler cultures use the
violence of metaphorical abstraction to excuse themselves from the real
work of decolonization: ensuring that land and power is in Indigenous
hands. Risling-Baldy's brilliant follow-up extends this logic to explain
how First People like Coyote have been reduced to metaphors through
settler appropriation. In both cases, [engagement with Indigenous
peoples and their relations masks moves to innocence: acts that make it
appear as if settlers are engaging in decolonization, while in fact we
are consolidating the power structures that privilege us.]{.underline}

In this series, want to show how [Western, and white-dominated,
[discourses on 'extinction']{.mark} [appear to address]{.mark} the
[systematic destruction]{.mark} of peoples and other beings [while
enacting moves to innocence]{.mark} [that mask]{.mark} their
[culpability]{.mark} [and perpetuate structures of
violence]{.mark}.]{.underline} As I argued in Part I of this series,
**[[extinction is an expression of colonial
violence]{.mark}.]{.underline}** As such, [it needs to be addressed
through direct **decolonization**, including the **dismantling** of
**settler colonial structures of violence**, and the resurgence of
Indigenous worlds.]{.underline} Following Tuck, Yang and Risling-Baldy's
lead, [I want to show how and why the violences that drive extinction
have come to be invisible within mainstream discourses.]{.underline}
[Salient amongst these is the practice of genocide against Indigenous
peoples other than humans.]{.underline}

...it is literally genocide.

[What Western science calls 'extinction']{.underline} is not an
unfortunate, unintended consequence of desirable 'human' activities. It
[is an embodiment of particular patterns of structural violence that
disproportionately affect specific racialized groups.]{.underline} [In
some cases,]{.underline} ['extinction' is directly, **deliberately** and
**systematically** **inflicted** in order to create space for
aggressors, including **settler states**.]{.underline} For this reason,
[it has rightly been framed as an aspect or tool of colonial genocides
against Indigenous human peoples.]{.underline} Indeed, many theorists
have shown that [the 'extirpation' of life forms]{.underline} (their
total removal from a particular place) [is an instrument for enacting
genocide upon Indigenous humans]{.underline} (see Mazis 2008; Laduke
1999; Stannard 1994). Specifically, the removal of key sources of food,
clothing and other basic materials makes survival on the land impossible
for the people targeted.

#### The plan reverses institutional failures that marginalize indigenous people in decision making processes 

**Lezak**, Scott Polar Research Institute, University of Cambridge,
**and Rock**, Resident at Native Village of Shaktoolik, **2024**

\[Stephen, Genevieve, "On Micropolitics: Climate adaptation and
Indigenous governance in Western Alaska," Climatic Change 177, 135
(2024). https://doi.org/10.1007/s10584-024-03769-4, HDCSW-KGrid
7/10/25\]

3.3 Discussion: improving domestic diplomacy

[Public institutions do not interact as sovereign bodies in a
frictionless medium.]{.underline} [There are forces at play that operate
at more mundane levels than policy itself.]{.underline} [**Governments**
are **not** things that can be **separated** from the complex world of
**human relationships**]{.underline}; they are made up of people,
computers, intermittent phone connections, hearing aids, and a whole
host of human and more-than-human components (Abrams 1988). The actual
practice of [how governments]{.underline}---federal, Tribal, state, and
regional---[communicate with each other is "messy"]{.underline} (Law
2004). And [**messiness is not apolitical**; it entrenches existing
power structures]{.underline}. [The neglect of
micropolitics]{.underline}, which is one form of this messiness, [leads
to **chronically poor relationships** and **ineffective communication**
between Alaska Native communities and their governments.]{.underline}

[Unaddressed, micropolitics may **prevent** Indigenous communities from
engaging on **equal footing** with the systems designed to serve them.
This **disparages** their **sovereignty**.]{.underline} [These
relationships]{.underline}, in order to succeed, [must be built on a
foundation of mutual trust and understanding]{.underline}. Potawatomi
scholar Kyle Whyte notes that "lessons from generations of colonialism
about how to avert bad relationship-making have not been learned by the
parties who should have learned them" (2020; p. 5). These [institutional
failures are not the responsibility of Tribes to address]{.underline}
(e.g., through "capacity building" as a self-contained remedy); [framing
them as such inappropriately accepts a logic that makes colonized
communities responsible for overcoming their own
marginalization]{.underline} (Kashwan and Ribot 2021).

It would be wrong to identify these issues as simple matters of policy
implementation. Rather, [these phenomena are **rooted** in deep
**structures of colonialism** and racism.]{.underline} The context of
"natural" disasters often gives false cover to the violent origins of
environmental vulnerability (Marino 2012; Marino et al. 2022). [In the
case of Alaska Native villages, their current precarity is not an issue
of bad geographical luck; rather, it's political all the way
down]{.underline}.

[[HR1 cuts]{.underline}]{.mark} the Department of Energy's Tribal Energy
Loan Guarantee Program, or [[TELGP]{.mark}.]{.underline} Initially
authorized for \$2 billion, the program's capacity swelled to \$20
billion under the Inflation Reduction Act.

The law also [rescinds any unobligated funding in the Environmental
Protection Agency]{.underline}'s Greenhouse Gas Reduction Fund, a \$27
billion Biden-era program created to [establish green banks and support
renewable energy projects in disadvantaged communities]{.underline}[.
[Those cuts]{.underline}]{.mark} [could further jeopardize ongoing
efforts to recover \$20 billion in clean-energy funding awarded under
the GGRF's National Clean Investment Fund ([NCIF]{.mark}) and Clean
Communities Investment Accelerator [(CCIA)]{.mark}
programs.]{.underline} In March, the EPA froze the funds --- including
accounts held at Citibank --- and formally terminated the grants. A U.S.
District Court quickly issued a temporary restraining order, finding the
EPA acted unlawfully. The EPA's attempt to overturn that ruling is still
awaiting a decision from the U.S. Court of Appeals.

In addition to cutting key tax credits and grant programs, [[HR 1
eliminates Department of Energy funding for grid transmission
expansion]{.mark} --- [a move that could strand tribal renewable
projects without access to markets]{.mark}. The bill also [imposes new
rental and capacity fees]{.mark} on wind and solar developments on
federal land, likely [raising the cost]{.mark} of doing business [for
tribes pursuing large-scale generation]{.mark}.]{.underline}

[[The cuts are part of a broader retreat from tribal clean energy
funding]{.underline}]{.mark} by Trump that began even before the
OBBBA\'s passage, with [some tribal projects already forced to seek
emergency philanthropic loans after federal grants were abruptly
frozen.]{.underline}

#### \*Unfortunately, these cuts come as summer ice melts in the Arctic -- Arctic plays a pivotal role in global climate -- multiple warrants require action to mitigate and adapt to climate change in the Arctic 

**Kayode-Edwards, et al., Researcher of ecotoxicology at Covenant
University, 24**

\[Ifeoluwa, "Climate Change in the Arctic", Arctic Marine Ecotoxicology,
December 6, 2024,
[[https://www.researchgate.net/profile/Kayode-Edwards-Ifeoluwa/publication/386474445_Climate_Change_in_the_Arctic/links/675821ff2c5fa80df7b5fc68/Climate-Change-in-the-Arctic.pdf]{.underline}](https://www.researchgate.net/profile/Kayode-Edwards-Ifeoluwa/publication/386474445_Climate_Change_in_the_Arctic/links/675821ff2c5fa80df7b5fc68/Climate-Change-in-the-Arctic.pdf),
Accessed July 14, HDCSW-MA.\]

[The [Arctic region plays a pivotal role in the global climate system
due to its unique geophysical and ecological
characteristics]{.mark}]{.underline}. [[The importance]{.mark} of the
Arctic [extends beyond its immediate geographic
boundaries]{.mark}]{.underline}[, [influencing weather patterns, oceanic
circulation, and global temperature regulation]{.underline}.]{.mark}
Understanding the Arctic's role in the climate system [is [crucial for
comprehending the broader impacts of climate change and developing
strategies to mitigate its effects]{.underline}]{.mark} \[70\]. [The
[Arctic's high albedo]{.mark}, or reflectivity, is a c[ritical]{.mark}
[factor in regulating the]{.mark} [Earth's energy
balance]{.mark}.]{.underline} Snow and ice surfaces reflect a
significant portion of incoming solar radiation back into space, helping
to cool the planet[[. As climate change leads to the melting of Arctic
ice, the albedo effect diminishes]{.underline},]{.mark} resulting in
increased absorption of solar radiation by darker ocean and land
surfaces. [[This positive feedback loop accelerates regional and global
warming]{.underline}]{.mark} \[38\]. [The ice-albedo feedback mechanism
is a [key]{.mark} [driver of Arctic amplification]{.mark}, where the
region warms at a faster rate than the global average]{.underline}. As
ice and snow melt, exposed surfaces absorb more heat, leading to further
melting and warming. [This process not only affects the Arctic but also
[contributes to global temperature rise and climate
variability]{.mark}]{.underline}. [[The Arctic is]{.underline}
[integral]{.underline}]{.mark} to the global thermohaline circulation,
also known as the [["ocean conveyor belt.]{.underline}"]{.mark} This
circulation is driven by differences in water density, influenced by
temperature and salinity. Cold, dense water in the Arctic sinks and
flows southward, driving deep ocean currents that help regulate global
climate by redistributing heat and nutrients. [[Disruptions to this

[behavior of the polar]{.mark} [jet stream]{.mark},]{.underline} a
fast-fowing ribbon of air that encircles the polar region. [Warming in

stream]{.underline}. This can [[result in extreme weather
events,]{.underline}]{.mark} such as prolonged cold spells, heatwaves,
and heavy precipitation, affecting midlatitude regions \[11\]. The
Arctic Oscillation (AO) is a climate pattern characterized by the
fluctuation of atmospheric pressure between the Arctic and
mid-latitudes. Changes in the AO can influence weather patterns across
the Northern Hemisphere. A positive phase of the AO is associated with a
stronger polar vortex and milder winter conditions in the mid-latitudes,
while a negative phase can lead to colder and more severe winters.
[[Arctic warming can impact the dynamics of the AO, contributing to
increased climate variability.]{.underline} The [Arctic is a significant

[from the Arctic]{.mark} has the potential to create a

[[thaws]{.underline}]{.mark}, these methane reserves can be released,

economic, and social well-being]{.mark}]{.underline}. Climate change and
its impacts on the Arctic environment threaten traditional livelihoods,
food security, and cultural heritage. [Understanding and addressing

sustainability of Arctic communities]{.underline}. [The Arctic is a
crucial component of the global climate system, influencing energy

An estimated [10 percent of the nearly four million residents throughout
the entire Arctic region are Indigenous]{.underline}.1) Indigenous
populations have inhabited much of the Arctic for thousands of years,
long before the land was claimed and divided into nation-states.
Interest in opportunities for natural resource extraction drove the
historic colonization in the Arctic2) and, [today, the
increasing]{.underline} [accessibility of minerals and oil due to
climate change is fuelling the]{.underline} so-called "**[Arctic
resource boom]{.underline}**"3)---[a quest to]{.underline} find and
**[exploit]{.underline}** [the region's remaining
resources.]{.underline} [As more international players hope to
participate in Arctic affairs, it is **critical** to not only consider
how these ventures impact **Indigenous populations** but to prioritize
**decolonization** in these spaces]{.underline}.

[Climate change is occurring more rapidly in the Arctic]{.underline}
than in any other part of the world, with the most recent evidence
pointing to a rate of warming nearly four times greater than the global
average.4) This [warming is driving the thawing of permafrost, loss of
seasonal snow cover, and melting of sea ice, ice caps and glaciers;
each,]{.underline} in turn, [increasing the **accessibility** of
**natural resources** and growing **global interest** in the
Arctic]{.underline}.5) This article uses a decolonization framework to
analyze resource development with a focus on the United States (US),
which is considered an Arctic state by way of Alaska. [Decolonization
will be defined as a concept and practice of confronting and reversing
the historic and ongoing legacies of colonialism,]{.underline}6)
[including the structures of power that uphold

After a brief background covering land ownership and some of the
outcomes of resource development in Alaska, this article will delve into
dynamics that reproduce and perpetuate colonial structures of power.
This analysis will feature (i) the prioritization of state and federal
interests through the idea of sovereignty and (ii) the primacy and
assumed superiority of Western knowledge and conventions and,
conversely, the disregard for Indigenous knowledge systems and
worldviews. To bridge the gap between theory and action, the article
will continually ask what it would mean to decolonize resource
development in the US Arctic. [**Confronting** the ongoing systems and
dynamics of **colonialism** must be placed at the **forefront** of
present-day and future Arctic engagement and
collaboration]{.underline}---not only in the US, but [throughout the
region]{.underline}

Background

The United States purchased the land that is present-day Alaska from
Russia in 1867. Just over a century later, in 1968, the discovery of oil
in Prudhoe Bay made the Arctic relevant at a national scale.7) The
commercial export of oil to the lower 48 states was now possible for the
first time. Hindering this ambition, however, was the fact that the
planned pipeline to carry the oil south traversed lands claimed by
Alaska Native populations. 'Alaska Native' refers collectively to the
Indigenous populations who have lived in present-day Alaska for
thousands of years before the arrival of Europeans in the 1700s.8) This
term encompasses "many nations and tribes" and "widely diverse cultures,
languages, lifeways, art forms and histories".9) To enable the
construction of the pipeline to the contiguous United States, land
claims issues around Prudhoe Bay were settled with the Alaska Native
Claims Settlement Act (ANCSA) in 1971. ANCSA extinguished Indigenous
claims to nearly 400 million acres of land throughout the state in
exchange for \$962.5 million and ownership of 43.7 million acres.

ANCSA also divided the state into twelve regions, each with an Alaska
Native Regional Corporation. The regional boundaries of the corporations
do not, however, equate to land ownership; land in Alaska is
predominantly owned by the state and federal governments. Just 10
percent of Alaska's 663,000 square miles are owned by Alaska Native
populations.10) The Regional Corporations operate under US corporate law
to administer resources and serve the local communities. These
for-profit corporations participate in the Alaskan natural resource
industry, both directly and indirectly.

[Federal Arctic policy in the US]{.underline} [has tended to
emphasize]{.underline} the [exploitation]{.underline} [of Arctic
resources for US economic development]{.underline}.11) W[ithin the new
National Strategy for the Arctic Region]{.underline} (released October
2022), [economic development continues to receive a strong
emphasis---alongside security---and Arctic investments are expected to
increase over the following decade]{.underline}.12) In the face of this
ever-growing interest in Arctic engagement, [Alaska Native
communities]{.underline} may [face new and renewed **colonial
intrusion** in the form of **large-scale resource
development**]{.underline}.13)

Effects of resource development

Continual '[**boom and bust**']{.underline} [with periods of financial
growth and decline characterizes resource development in the
**capitalist system**]{.underline}.14) This [instability]{.underline}
[prohibits]{.underline} many [communities from relying]{.underline}
solely [on economies centered on resource extraction]{.underline}.
[Subsistence practices provide a source of greater
stability]{.underline} within what would otherwise be an unstable
extractive economy.15) [**Resource extraction** has]{.underline},
however, [had **detrimental impacts** on the land, water, and wildlife
that reduce the viability of subsistence lifeways.]{.underline}
Specifically, [extractive activities have been found to **increase**
**vulnerability** to **biological invasion**]{.underline},16) [bring
changes to the land with built infrastructure]{.underline},17) [and
introduce the risk of contamination, such as **oil
spills**]{.underline}.18) [The resulting decreased viability of
subsistence practices is worsened by the threat climate change poses to
hunting and gathering activities]{.underline}.19)

Although some measures of physical health have improved since ANCSA,20)
[resource extraction can compromise the **health** and **wellbeing** of
nearby Indigenous populations.]{.underline} [Significant health
disparities remain among Alaska Natives compared to
non-native]{.underline} Alaskans and other US [residents]{.underline}.
Generally, [Alaska Natives have a **lower life expectancy**, increased
risk of **cardiovascular disease**, and an **elevated burden of
infectious disease**.]{.underline}21) [By exacerbating **food
insecurity**, the negative impacts of resource development may
contribute to and **worsen health outcomes**.]{.underline}
[Many]{.underline} of these disparities [are rooted in]{.underline} the
history of [colonialism]{.underline} [and]{.underline} [socio-political
marginalization]{.underline}; in addition, [their persistence today may
be attributed to the disproportionate exposure to environmental health
hazards]{.underline}.22) Moreover, some researchers have found that
[health data fails to produce a holistic picture of well-being,
suggesting that there must be a greater consideration for
context-specific understandings of health status]{.underline}.23)

Importantly, [the effects of resource development are not felt uniformly
by Alaska Natives]{.underline}. Instead, [various facets of identity
among individuals and communities impact how they relate to extractive
activities]{.underline}. [Socioeconomic class divisions]{.underline},24)
[gender identity]{.underline},25) [one's location in a rural versus
urban setting]{.underline},26) [and many other factors can all impact
how resource development is experienced]{.underline}. [The
**complexity** of the relationship]{.underline} between resource
development and Indigenous populations, and the diversity within the
Alaska Native population, [**must not be ignored** or
simplified]{.underline}.

Introduction

[[The **Arctic** is experiencing the **fastest warming in the
world**]{.underline}]{.mark}, warming [[at a rate]{.underline} [nearly
four times faster than the global average]{.underline},]{.mark} a
phenomenon [[known as **Arctic Amplification**]{.underline}]{.mark}
\[••85\], [with]{.underline} potential [i[mpacts]{.mark} **[beyond its
borders]{.mark}**]{.underline} \[••74\]. [[Climate change is having
**widespread im- pacts** on ecosystem services and communities,
including **sea ice loss**, increasing risk of **mass casualty**
incidents, **rising temperature**, **thawing permafrost**, **food
insecurity**, and im- pacts on **livelihoods** and culture of
**Indigenous commu- nities**]{.underline}]{.mark} \[••14,••2,66\]. [The
Arctic has a population of]{.underline} approximately [4
million]{.underline} people, of whom approximately [10% are
Indigenous]{.underline}, living in Alaska, Canada, Finland, Greenland,
Norway, Russia, and Sweden \[••65\]. [[In- digenous Peoples have
historically adapted through]{.underline}]{.mark} the combination of
[[traditional knowledge, sharing networks, mobility, and
technology]{.underline} \[70\] [yet face **significant con- straints**
in a contemporary context]{.underline}]{.mark} \[22\].

[[Barriers are **systemic obstacles** that **hamper progress** to- wards
effective adaptation but **can be overcome**]{.underline}]{.mark}
\[98\]. Limits are thresholds, inherent restrictions, and points beyond
which social or ecological systems cannot adapt without significant
transformation or collapse or beyond which no more adaptation is
possible \[40,••45\]. [Barriers and limits to adaptation
have]{.underline} largely [been associated with a range of
socioeconomic, cultural, institutional, and biophysical factors and
are]{.underline} strongly [influenced by the rates at which
climate-influenced risks emerge and the pace at which adaptive changes
can be made]{.underline} \[••3,62,64,67,98\].

Barriers and limits to adaptation in the Arctic are not entirely
negative; while many restrict adaptation, others serve protective
socioecological functions. [**Environmental limits**, such as extreme
cold, permafrost, and remoteness, **discourage** large-scale
**industrialisation** and mass in-mi- gration, sustaining Arctic
ecosystems and Indigenous subsistence practices]{.underline} \[49\].
[[Regulatory barriers]{.underline}]{.mark}, like wild- life protection
laws and fishing quotas, prevent over- harvesting and support long-term
ecological sustainability \[105\], though they [[can become restrictive
when imposed without Indigenous input or community
engagement]{.underline}.]{.mark}

While studies have extensively documented climate change impacts and
adaptation strategies in the Arctic \[18,27,34,36,49\], less attention
has been given to iden- tifying barriers and limits to adaptation,
particularly for Indigenous communities. This paper aims to fulfil this
gap by identifying and characterising potential barriers and limits to
adaptation for Indigenous Peoples in the Arctic. This study illustrates
the significance of these [[barriers and limits]{.underline}]{.mark}
through selected examples that highlight their diverse manifestations
across Arctic regions. These examples [[illustrate the complexity of
adaptation challenges]{.underline}]{.mark}, providing a framework for
ana- lysing barriers and limits in specific geographic and so-
ciopolitical contexts. This paper examines how [social norms, individual
values, psychosocial factors, and [gov- ernance systems create barriers
and limits across Arctic regions]{.mark}]{.underline}[,]{.mark}
following the structure outlined in Table 1.

[Many national [governments prioritise **neoliberal devel- opment** and
**resource extraction**]{.mark}]{.underline} (mining, oil, and gas),
[[while **Indigenous governance** systems advocate for **sus- tainable
resource management**]{.underline}]{.mark} \[103,••32,92\]. In Norway,
the government's focus on oil and gas development often conflicts with
Indigenous demands for sustainable re- source management \[33\]. [[This
prioritisation of resource extraction over environmental and cultural
preservation acts as a barrier to adaptation]{.underline}]{.mark}. In
Russia, large-scale oil and gas projects --- such as those in the Yamal

traditional migration routes \[107,44\]. [In Alaska and Canada,

disrupt wildlife migration patterns, and accelerate per- mafrost thawing
\[31\]. However, the project was still ap- proved, highlighting how
governance systems frequently privilege economic interests over
Indigenous sovereignty and long-term climate resilience.

Indigenous communities have permanently lost land, mobility, and

Canada and [Alaska]{.mark}, many [Indigenous communities were forcibly
relocated to permanent settlements, disrupting lifestyles, tradi- tional

have had lasting im- pacts on food security, mental health, and cultural
pre- servation, creating a structural limit to adaptation]{.underline}
\[25\].]{.mark}

[[Colonialism and injustice have evolved into government policies
limiting access to resources]{.underline}]{.mark} such as small-scale
Indigenous Peoples' fisheries and favouring offshore traw- lers,

\[94\]. [Historical, interpersonal, epistemic, and systemic racism has

[This presents a limit and undermines the adaptive
capacity]{.underline}.

[[**Colonisation** has systematically **turned** the natural con-
straints in the Arctic into **severe socioeconomic barriers** by
**undermining** Indigenous **autonomy** and **severing traditional
adaptation strategies**]{.underline}]{.mark} \[••65\]. Forced se-
dentarisation, land dispossession, and restrictive re- source governance
have stripped Indigenous communities of their ability to flexibly
respond to en- vironmental challenges \[104\]. [[Colonial policies have
re- inforced]{.underline} the [infrastructural
limitations]{.underline}]{.mark} [--- [investing in resource extraction
rather than local development, leaving Indigenous communities with high
costs of living, food insecurity, and limited access to essential
service]{.mark}s]{.underline} \[55,90\]. [[What were once manageable
ecological limits have thus been compounded by imposed social,
political, and economic constraints, shaping the struc- tural
inequalities that define contemporary Arctic adap- tation
challenges]{.underline}.]{.mark}

[Colonialism has created both barriers and limits to adaptation by
forcibly relocating Indigenous commu- nities to environmentally
unsuitable and high-risk areas]{.underline}, where traditional hunting
and fishing practices are no longer viable. While initially a barrier,
this [displacement has evolved into a structural limit, as these
communities are now deeply established, making relocation im- practical
despite increasing climate risks]{.underline}. State-imposed wildlife
regulations restrict Indigenous adaptive flex- ibility, preventing
communities from adjusting their subsistence practices in response to
environmental changes \[22\]. These [[policies]{.underline}, often
[designed without Indigenous input]{.underline}, [reinforce colonial
constraints on adaptation, further limiting the agency of Arctic In-
digenous Peoples in navigating climate challenges]{.underline}]{.mark}.

The [[geopolitical competition]{.underline}]{.mark} among major powers
such as the United States, Russia, Canada, and China; the [[esca- lation
of military operations]{.underline} by Arctic nations; the ex[- pansion
of commercial shipping routes through the Arctic]{.underline}; [and the
exploration of oil, gas, and mineral resources]{.underline} [have
emerged as important limits affecting Arctic governance and
adaptation]{.underline} \[]{.mark}30,79\]. The geopolitics of the Arctic
has gained global attention due to climate change, resulting in
increased interest in commercial shipping and energy resources \[95\].
The reduction of sea ice has triggered a surge in human activities where
countries and corpora- tions vie for control over resources and shipping
routes, diverting attention and resources for adaptation. This is
exacerbated by increasing militarisation of the Arctic \[101\],
affecting environmental and human sustainability and security and
long-term adaptation planning. Russia has significantly expanded
military bases in the Arctic, while the U.S. and NATO allies have
increased defence spending \[57\]. This geopolitical tension reduces
interna- tional cooperation on climate adaptation, as nations prioritise
strategic control over adaptation funding and environmental protection.
Geopolitical competition is a structural limit, as it shifts governance
priorities away from climate resilience and human security and toward
national security concerns.

Conclusion

[[Addressing barriers and limits to climate change adap- tation requires
transformative structural changes that recognise Indigenous rights,
knowledge, and sover- eignty, and a move towards **inclusive
governance**]{.underline}]{.mark}. [[These changes can be achieved
through targeted policies and frameworks]{.underline},]{.mark} such as
the Arctic Council's Sustainable Development Working Group, [which
facilitates colla- boration on climate resilience strategies grounded in
Indigenous knowledge.]{.underline} [Initiatives like co-management
agreements between Indigenous communities and na- tional governments
provide effective models for fos- tering inclusive
decision-making.]{.underline} [[Investing in **resilient
infrastructure**, promoting **Indigenous knowledge sys- tems**, and
ensuring **social** and **economic equity** are im- portant steps toward
**enhancing adaptation capacity** and **long-term sustainability** in
the Arctic]{.underline}.]{.mark}

Conceptualizing Indigenous sovereignty

[Notions of Indigenous sovereignty are connected to]{.underline} the
right to [self-determination]{.underline}. [[Decolonization
must]{.underline} [seek the conceptualization and expression of
Indigenous sovereignty **outside** of the **western hegemony** and
enduring **colonial structures**]{.underline}; [self-determination and
sovereignty are limited when they can only be articulated within the
existing capitalist order]{.underline}]{.mark}.39) Dr. Melanie K. Yazzie
presents the idea of [[anti-capitalist decolonization]{.underline} to
[challenge the "hegemonic formation of 'extractivism' and its liberal,
capitalist, heteropatriarchal, and settler colonial valance of
development"]{.underline}.40) [This approach to decolonization seems
especially well suited to the Alaskan context as it critiques how
resource development is carried out in a capitalist system]{.underline},
[instead of presenting resource extraction as]{.underline}]{.mark}
something [[inherently at odds with indigeneity]{.mark}.]{.underline}
Indigenous sovereignty is reliant on the ability of communities to have
autonomy over their resources and decision-making, as they have for
thousands of years.41) [[An anti-capitalist approach to decolonization
offers a way to center Indigenous epistemologies and enable economic,
societal, cultural, and political pathways conceptualized and
implemented by Alaska Native people themselves]{.underline}]{.mark}.

The ideas presented throughout this article are applicable well beyond
the US Arctic, and beyond the realm of resource development. [[Interest
in **exploiting natural resources** drove historic **colonization**
throughout the **entire Arctic region** and the lingering structures and
systems of power]{.mark} continue to shape Indigenous lives and limit
the capacity of communities and individuals to mold their own
destinies]{.underline}. Therefore, [[interrogating and confronting the
ongoing systems and dynamics of colonialism **must be prioritized**
within Arctic engagement and collaboration going
forward]{.underline}]{.mark}. In light of the new National Strategy for
the Arctic Region---which differs, in one way, from the prior 2013
edition in making no mention of oil or gas42)---[there is a need for
further research and analysis to identify]{.underline} the implications
of this new strategy on [efforts to decolonize and achieve Indigenous
sovereignty]{.underline}[. [There is also a need to **center** and
**privilege Indigenous voices** in this space and, as such, **future
research** must be **informed** and **conceptualized** by and for
**Arctic Indigenous populations**]{.underline}]{.mark}.

#### \*Current approaches to development create green sacrifice zones that ignore input of indigenous groups
