## Impact -- climate change bad -- famine 

### Internal link -- adaptation 

#### Lack of integration of indigenous knowledge creates food shortages 

**Malik,** School of Geography, University of Leeds UK, **and Ford,**
Priestley Centre for Climate Futures UK, **25**

**\[Ishfaq Hussain Malik, and James D. Ford, "**Barriers and limits to
adaptation in the Arctic", [[Current Opinion in Environmental
Sustainability]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/journal/current-opinion-in-environmental-sustainability),
[[https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#ab0015]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#ab0015) 
April 2025, access date: 7/8/2025\] HDCSW LN 

**[Social norms tied to traditional subsistence practices are being
fundamentally disrupted by climate change, leading to limits on
adaptation.]{.underline}** An important example of this limit is the
**[loss of key wildlife species essential to Indigenous diets and
cultural identity]{.underline}**. The decline of caribou populations in
the Canadian Arctic has reached a point where some **[communities can no
longer rely on this culturally and nutritionally important species
[8](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib8),
[93](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib93).
This represents a limit to adaptation, as the loss of caribou undermines
food security, cultural identity, and traditional practices,
significantly impacting Inuit communities.]{.underline}** Conservation
efforts, such as restricting caribou hunting, address ecological
sustainability but force communities to abandon traditional food-sharing
networks, important for survival and social cohesion
[[\[42\]]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib42).
In Greenland, Inuit hunters rely on sea ice for seal and whale hunting,
which is economically and culturally significant. However, **[with sea
ice loss accelerating, these traditional hunting grounds are
disappearing
[\[37\]](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib37).
This represents a limit]{.underline}** --- without ice, these practices
cannot continue. **[This leads to both food insecurity and cultural
erosion.]{.underline}**

## Impact -- climate change -- flooding 

### Empirics 

#### Climate change induced flooding and wildfires proves climate crisis now 

**Niaz**, writer, **25**

\[Mehmood, "Is Climate Change Reaching a Tipping Point? 2025 Wildfires &
Floods"Voice Earth, 6/2/25,
https://vocal.media/earth/is-climate-change-reaching-a-tipping-point-2025-wildfires-and-floods,HDCSW-KGrid
7/15/25\]

In recent months, the [planet has sent us some of its loudest warnings
yet. From wildfires raging across Southern Europe and North America to
devastating floods sweeping through South Asia and parts of Africa, 2025
has become a year marked by extreme climate event]{.underline}s. The
question on everyone's mind now is sobering but necessary: Are we
[nearing the tipping point of climate change]{.underline}? This question
is no longer just for scientists or politicians to answer. It's a
reality that farmers, families, children, and entire communities are now
experiencing firsthand. [The climate crisis is no longer about the
future. It is happening now.]{.underline} Wildfires That Refuse to Be
Contained In the summer of 2025, wildfires have erupted across many
regions with shocking speed and strength. Canada has faced one of its
worst fire seasons on record, with millions of acres of forest land
burned and entire towns evacuated. In California and Oregon, flames
swallowed entire neighborhoods, displacing thousands of residents and
releasing dangerous amounts of carbon into the atmosphere. What [makes
these fires more intense than before? Rising global temperatures,
combined with longer drought seasons and lower humidity, have created
ideal conditions for fire to thrive.]{.underline} These are not ordinary
wildfires. They are fast-moving, unpredictable, and much harder to
control than in past years. In many cases, firefighters are simply
trying to redirect the flames rather than stop them. What's most
heartbreaking is that behind each statistic are people --- mothers
carrying children through smoke-filled streets, elderly citizens losing
homes they built decades ago, and workers risking their lives to save
others. This isn't just a climate crisis. It's a human crisis.
Floodwaters That Rise Too Fast While some regions burn, others drown. In
early 2025, Bangladesh was hit by its worst flooding in over a decade.
Rivers overflowed with such force that entire villages vanished
overnight. Streets turned into rivers, homes into floating debris. In
parts of Pakistan and India, monsoon rains came earlier and harder than
usual, affecting millions and damaging vital crops. [Even in places not
traditionally known for flooding --- like parts of Germany, the United
States, and South Korea --- urban flash floods have become frighteningly
common.]{.underline} These floods are not just caused by heavy rain but
by poor urban planning and outdated infrastructure that cannot handle
modern-day climate stress. [The link between these floods and climate
change is clear. Warmer temperatures hold more moisture in the
atmosphere, leading to heavier downpours. Melting glaciers and rising
sea levels also contribute to flooding, particularly in low-lying
region]{.underline}s.

#### Hydroclimate whiplash causing more flooding 

Waterman, Author at Climate Adaptation Center, 25

\[Stu, "A Hotter World Is Inevitable: Part 1", Climate Adaptation
Center, May 3 2025,
[[https://www.theclimateadaptationcenter.org/2025/05/03/a-hotter-world-is-inevitable-part-1/]{.underline}](https://www.theclimateadaptationcenter.org/2025/05/03/a-hotter-world-is-inevitable-part-1/),
access date: 7/14/25, HDCSW SZ\]

[The NOAA National Centers for Environmental Information]{.underline}
(NCEI) [report that the 2024 average temperature across the contiguous
United States was 55.5°F (13°C). This is 3.5°F (1.9 °C) above the
20th-century average, making it the warmest year on record since
measurements began in 1895. The year got off to a horrific start with
the catastrophic wildfires surrounding Los Angeles, boosted by a
phenomenon called hydroclimate whiplash---rapid swings between intensely
wet and intensely dry weather.]{.underline} The wet period leads to
rapid and abundant growth of shrubs and grasses, which become tinder-dry
in subsequent hot, dry weather.

[Hydroclimate whiplash set the stage for the Los Angeles
fires]{.underline}: multiple atmospheric rivers in the winter of
2022-2023 dumped rain and heavy snowfall, only to be followed by the
record hot, dry summer of 2024. [Wildfires were virtually inevitable at
that point and strong Santa Ana winds drove the fires to spread rapidly.
As the global climate warms, a recent by UCLA's]{.underline} Daniel
Swain and colleagues [shows that hydroclimate whiplash has increased by
31% to 66% since the mid-twentieth century. Climate change is powering
the increase, such that it will more than double should global
temperatures rise to 3°C above pre-industrial levels.]{.underline}
Beyond the U.S., the impacts of climate warming are increasingly
evident.

[In mid-April,]{.underline} for example, [a severe heatwave hit India
and Pakistan.]{.underline} New Delhi reported multiple incidences of
temperatures exceeding 40°C (104°F), as much as 5°C above the seasonal
average. Parts of Pakistan experienced temperatures up to 49°C (120°F).
[The analysts conclude that the April heatwave was driven by "very
exceptional meteorological conditions whose characteristics can mostly
be ascribed to human driven climate change. As an inevitable consequence
of a warming climate---both in the atmosphere and in the
oceans---tropical cyclone intensity has increased around the
world]{.underline} over approximately the past 40 years. [In the
Atlantic Basin, the National Hurricane Center suggests that the overall
frequency of intense hurricanes, specifically major hurricanes (Category
3 or higher), has increased in recent decades. Recent NOAA
research shows that, while the overall number of hurricanes in a season
may not change drastically over the long term, the likelihood of
extremely active seasons is increasing. ]{.underline}

### Melting ice 

#### Ice melting increase risk of flooding and killing marine organisms 

**Otsuki**,Graduate School of Fisheries Sciences, Hokkaido University,
**and ,Sugiyama** Graduate School of Fisheries Sciences, Hokkaido
University **25**

Mayuko and Sugiyama,"Community perspectives inform coastal marine
ecosystem research in northwestern Greenland" , Elsvier, June
2025,file:///C:/Users/Nboestudent/Downloads/Community-perspectives-inform-coastal-marine-ecosystem-researc_2025_Polar-Sc.pdf
, 7-9-2025, HDSW-JP

**[Climate change affects the Arctic environment and organisms within
it, including humans.]{.underline} In [Greenland, the ice sheet and
peripheral glaciers are rapidly losing mass, resulting in sea level rise
and changes in ocean environments]{.underline}** (Box et al., 2022;
Smith et al., 2020). In northwestern Greenland, **[natural hazards
triggered by climate change have been observed]{.underline}** for at
least the last decade. For example, **l[andslides occurred after
heavy]{.underline}** **[precipitation]{.underline}** in 2016 and 2017,
which damaged a building in Siorapaluk (Sugiyama, 2020; Watanabe et al.,
2022). In 2015 and 2016, because of high air temperatures and heavy
rain, increased discharge from a glacier caused flooding in Qaanaaq.
This resulted in the destruction of a road and collapse of a bridge
connecting the village to Qaanaaq Airport (Kondo et al., 2021). Similar
incidents happened again in 2023 **[due to heavy
precipitation]{.underline}** (Fig. 1a). **[Climate change is a common
link between these events, and rainfall has been increasing in
Greenlan]{.underline}**d (Box et al., 2023). Moreover, the mean air
temperature in Qaanaaq during the summer months of June--August has
increased by more than 1 ◦C in the last two decades (Wang et al., 2021).
**[Climate change affects the distribution of organisms including a
trending poleward shift of subarctic species habitats]{.underline}**
(Wassmann et al., 2011)[. Another consequence of increasing glacier
melting and increased precipitation is the greater amounts of
runoff]{.underline} along the coast of Qaanaaq. During the **[flood
event in 2023, turbid water discharge from a glacial river was observed
along the coast near the village]{.underline}** (Fig. 1b). Such an event
might have affected the dump site, which is also located on the coast
(Fig. 1c). Additionally, river runoff controls the amount of mercury
discharge into the ocean (Hawkings et al., 2021) although the
significance of mercury discharge from the Greenland Ice sheet is
controversial (Jørgensen et al., 2024). In northwestern **[Greenland,
residents hunt and consume marine mammals and fish]{.underline}**
(Nuttall, 2020). T**[herefore, it is important to understand the ecology
of high trophic level species, because heavy metals bioaccumulate in
marine organisms.]{.underline}** Under the framework of Japanese
multidisciplinary Arctic research projects, ArCS (Arctic Challenge for
Sustainability, 2015--2020) and ArCS II (2020--2025), we continue to
study the coastal marine ecosystem in the Gulf of Inglefield,
northwestern Greenland. The objective of this research is to understand
the ecology of marine organisms and their interplay with human and
rapidly changing environments (Sugiyama et al., 2021). Our research
activities include: acoustic monitoring of marine mammals (Podolskiy and
Sugiyama, 2020), fish and zooplankton; contaminants affecting seabirds
(Otsuki et al., 2024) and marine mammals; diet and foraging ecology of
marine mammals (Sakuragi et al., 2024); distribution of plankton
(Matsuno et al., 2020; Naito et al., 2019), the ecology of seabirds
(Nishizawa et al., 2019) and marine mammals. In addition to the research
activities, we have organized workshops with the residents in Qaanaaq
from 2016 to 2019, as well as in 2022 and 2023, and in Qeqertat in 2022
to introduce our project, share study results, and discuss the needs and
future potential research interests that the residents may have. The
workshops created an excellent opportunity for interacting with the
residents, and helped to build trust between the residents and
researchers. This enabled us to develop our research activities in a
tansdisciplinary approach.

## Impact -- climate change bad -- health 

#### Climate change in the Arctic intensifies health risks---escalating injury, disease, and mortality, especially for Indigenous communities.

**Rao et al, Senior Researcher, 25**

\[Shilpa, "Climate change and health in the Arctic", Scandinavian
Journal of Public Health, March 13, 2025,
[[https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1177/14034948241296309]{.underline}](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1177/14034948241296309),
access date: 7/13/25, HDCSW SZ\]

[Direct health effects of climate change may result from increased
exposure to extreme weather events and from extreme events such as
falling through ice.]{.underline} [When one part of the Arctic warms,
the strength and direction of wind and water currents can alter,
resulting in a decrease in temperature elsewhere in the
Arctic.]{.underline} [Thus,]{.underline} climate impacts may be neither
linear nor smooth. In the Arctic, the rise in heat extremes and decrease
in cold extremes especially pronounced, in association with
intensification of the hydrological cycle and other changes. [Climate
change is likely to have both positive and negative direct health
impacts on health in the Arctic.]{.underline}

Mortality rates from unintentional injuries are already
disproportionately high in Arctic regions, especially for Indigenous
peoples. [Rates of drowning in Inuit populations have been reported as
six times higher than for non-Indigenous populations, and eight times
higher for snowmobile-related drownings. Hospitalization rates for
unintentional injuries from land transportation in areas with high Inuit
populations are greater for adults, children and youth. Climate change
is expected to significantly impact weather conditions in the future and
thus increase this form of burden for Arctic populations.]{.underline}
[The Arctic has long been an area at high risk]{.underline} from
ultra-violet (UV) radiation damage.

Highly reflective snow surfaces result [in damaging levels of UV to
unprotected eyes and vertical surfaces]{.underline} including faces,
trees and shrubs. [High UV levels in the Arctic are known to cause
sunburn (erythema) and snow blindness (photokeratitis) under normal
conditions. UV is an identified immune suppressant, with long term
health consequences including cataracts and skin diseases, including
cancer.]{.underline} Normally, ozone in the stratosphere shields the
Earth from much harmful UV radiation. However, [recent measurements in
the Arctic show that climate change is contributing to long-term
decreases in the amount of ozone high overhead]{.underline}. In
addition, scientists have observed more frequent episodes of extremely
low ozone levels, particularly during the springtime, due to climate
change. [The fragile ecosystems of the Arctic, with its peoples and
human-constructed surfaces, may thus face additional challenges due to
ozone depletion associated modifications to UV-B and UV-A
radiation.]{.underline}

## Impact -- climate change bad -- heat deaths 

\*means need adaptation

#### Increase in heat deaths from prolonged heat waves inevitable in the squo 

Waterman, Author at Climate Adaptation Center, 25

\[Stu, "A Hotter World Is Inevitable: Part 2", Climate Adaptation
Center, May 18 2025,
[[https://www.theclimateadaptationcenter.org/2025/05/18/a-hotter-world-is-inevitable-part-2/]{.underline}](https://www.theclimateadaptationcenter.org/2025/05/18/a-hotter-world-is-inevitable-part-2/),
access date: 7/14/25, HDCSW SZ\]

First and foremost, many of us can expect escalating heat and
unrelenting heat waves. [The CAC took an in depth look at future warming
and heatwaves three years ago]{.underline} (Overheated: Summer
2002, Part 1 and Part 2). At that time we suggested that climate warming
might reach as much as 3°C by 2100. [The World Health Organization found
that between 2000 and 2016, the number of people exposed to heatwaves
increased by around 125 million. The greatest risk lies in countries in
or near the tropics, where temperatures already approach human limits.
Conversely, higher latitudes, will benefit from a decrease in
cold-temperature deaths as the climate warms. In the U.S., more
than 21,000 heat-related deaths occurred from 1999 through 2023, with
the annual death toll more than doubling over the 24 years.]{.underline}

[A study of Europe's 2023 heat wave found that it resulted in more than
47,000 deaths. Until recently, the theoretical limit was thought to be a
35°C "wet-bulb temperature"]{.underline}[, equivalent to 35°C (95°F) at
100% humidity or 46°C (115°F) at 50% humidity. However, ]{.mark}[recent
research at Penn State University showed that the wet-bulb threshold is
actually lower --- about 31°C (87°F) at 100% humidity for healthy, young
adults.]{.underline} []{.mark}[By 2050, the global population over 60
years old is forecast to be about 2.1 billion]{.underline}, roughly 21%
of the total[. Research]{.underline} by Giacomo Falchetta and colleagues
[predicts that as a result of rising temperatures, the number of people
at risk worldwide from chronic extreme heat will at least double by
2050, with the population of older people regularly exposed to both
chronic and acute heat increasing by about 200 million.]{.underline}

## Impact -- climate change bad -- indigenous culture 

### Ecological grief 

#### Climate induced ecological grief creates intergenerational harm for indigenous people 

**Malik,** School of Geography, University of Leeds UK, **and Ford,**
Priestley Centre for Climate Futures UK, **25**

**\[**Ishfaq Hussain Malik, and James D. Ford, **"**Barriers and limits
to adaptation in the Arctic", [[Current Opinion in Environmental
Sustainability]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/journal/current-opinion-in-environmental-sustainability),
[[https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#ab0015]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#ab0015) 
April 2025, access date: 7/8/2025\] HDCSW LN 

 Psychosocial factors such as cultural alienation, mental health
struggles, intergenerational disconnect, and loss of trust in
institutions shape how Arctic Indigenous communities perceive, engage
with, and respond to climate change. **[Indigenous communities face
historical trauma of forced displacement and cultural
alienation]{.underline}**
[[\[88\]]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib88).
[Many Indigenous communities are facing the threat of climate-induced
relocation, as coastal erosion, permafrost thawing, and extreme weather
events threaten settlements that have existed for generations
[12](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib12),
[51](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib51).
However, relocation is not just a physical challenge --- it represents a
rupture in cultural continuity, identity, and psychological
well-being]{.underline}. In Alaska, the communities of Shishmaref,
Newtok, and Kivalina, are facing forced relocation due to rising sea
levels and coastal erosion
[[25]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib25),
[[9]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib9).
For many community members, **[this displacement represents more than
just losing a home --- it means losing a way of life, severing their
spiritual and cultural ties to the land
[\[71\]](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib71).]{.underline}**
In Siberia, Indigenous Evenki and Nenets herders face disruptions to
traditional migration routes due to industrial expansion and resource
extraction
[[\[96\]]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib96).
These changes force some herders into fixed settlements, where they
experience high levels of psychological distress and increased
dependency on state welfare system**[s]{.underline}**
[[\[52\]]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib52).
[This psychological trauma represents an adaptation limit --- once the
connection to land is severed, a fundamental part of
Indigenous]{.underline} [identity is lost. Socioeconomic inequalities,
poverty, income disparities, and access to resources create barriers and
lead to lower social well-being, affecting access to health care, food,
housing, basic needs, education, and skill development in the
Arctic]{.underline}
[[16]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib16),
[[39]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib39),
[[6]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib6),
[[83]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib83).
**[Traditional livelihoods are increasingly becoming unsustainable and
creating barriers for economically disadvantaged households dependent on
them]{.underline}**
[[80]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib80),
[[89]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib89).
These inequities are exacerbated by systemic issues such as social
marginalisation, inadequate infrastructure, and limited government
support (at national to regional levels). **[Climate change has affected
social networks by impacting hunting and herding, availability of
traditional foods, and high costs associated with accessing these
foods]{.underline}**
[[38••]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib38),
[[50]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib50).
This has been complicated by a reduction in the number of wildlife that
are culturally important to the communities. [Food insecurity is a
growing crisis in many Arctic regions, exacerbating mental health
issues]{.underline}
[[\[56\]]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib56).
For example, the decline in the number of caribou in the Canadian Arctic
has affected food security, traditional knowledge, mental health,
cultural continuity, and the transmission of skills across the
generations, compounded by the high costs of substitute foods
[[\[8\]]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib8).
[This loss of communal food security creates chronic stress, anxiety,
and depression,]{.underline} particularly for older generations who
struggle to adjust to these shifts
[[\[72\]]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib72).
[These mental health challenges act as a barrier to
adaptation]{.underline}, as they reduce the capacity of individuals and
communities to respond effectively to environmental changes. Indigenous
communities face high rates of diabetes, cardiovascular disease, and
mental health issues, largely due to nutritional transitions from
traditional diets to processed store-bought foods and financial
constraints
[[99]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib99),
[[58]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib58).
These health issues reduce the adaptive capacity of individuals. This
represents both a barrier and a limit[. Food insecurity and health
disparities that arise from climate-induced changes in wildlife
availability represent limits]{.underline}, particularly for
subsistence-dependent communities. Many Indigenous Peoples experience a
sense of loss, anxiety, and uncertainty about the future
[[\[56\]](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib56)**.
In the North American Arctic, the loss of sea ice has led to 'ecological
grief', where individuals mourn the disappearance of traditional
landscapes, loss of cultural practices, and uncertainty about their
community's long-term survival**
[\[15\]](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1877343525000120#bib15)]{.underline}.
This psychological burden represents an adaptation limit, as policy
interventions cannot reverse existential grief tied to irreversible
ecological loss. 

### food 

#### Climate change intensifies Indigenous food security in the Arctic through species decline, disrupted hunting patterns, and threats to reindeer and marine mammal populations.

Rao et al, Senior Researcher, 25

\[Shilpa, "Climate change and health in the Arctic", Scandinavian
Journal of Public Health, March 13, 2025,
[[https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1177/14034948241296309]{.underline}](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1177/14034948241296309),
access date: 7/13/25, HDCSW SZ\]

The traditional diet varies among the Arctic nations. [In Canada,
Greenland, Alaska and the far-east of the Russian Federation there is
significant marine mammal consumption by Indigenous
peoples.]{.underline} However[, in some parts of the Arctic --
particularly Finland, Sweden, Norway and Arctic Russia -- there is
significant hunting and consumption of terrestrial mammals. Furthermore,
reindeer herding is an important livelihood and reindeer meat
constitutes a significant amount of the diet of the indigenous
population of Saami people]{.underline} in Norway, Sweden, Finland and
Russia. [Fish consumption is also a significant part of the diet among
both Indigenous and non-Indigenous peoples in many Arctic countries.
However, Arctic populations have been moving towards store-bought
'Western' foods for decades owing to greater accessibility,
affordability and also concerns about the health impacts from
contaminants in traditional and local foods. Higher reliance on imported
foods and higher cost of healthy foods contribute to unhealthy
diets.]{.underline}

The details and pace of these nutritional transitions vary across and
within the Arctic countries, but in general the shifts have been
greatest in urban areas and towns. [Such dietary changes may have
negative and positive impacts; their balance is often difficult to
ascertain. The benefits of diminished consumption of traditional and
local foods include significant reductions of contaminants measured in
the blood of pregnant women. Increased consumption of vegetables and
fruit can also occur in some cases. At the same time, studies have noted
troubling trends in deterioration of health status, including a rising
prevalence of obesity, diabetes and other metabolic diseases. These
harms are triggered not only by increased intake of calorie-dense,
nutrient-poor foods, but also by reduced physical activity as the
incentive to hunt declines.]{.underline}

[A deterioration in dental health has also been observed, related to
higher consumption of sugary drinks, while the decline in
cardiometabolic health is related to reduced consumption of fish and
other traditional foods]{.underline} and consequently lower intakes of
omega-3-fatty acids, vitamin D and iodine. [The consequences of climate
change pose several threats to food systems and food security in the
Arctic region. Rising sea levels, changes in wind and precipitation
patterns, and more unpredictable and severe weather events have been
documented to result in a decline in fish stocks, birds and marine
mammals; species that are traditionally and culturally important, both
as food, and for human identity in the Arctic region]{.underline}.

[Climate change-induced increases in biting pests, such as mosquitos,
may also have negative consequences for reindeer and caribou
populations, and accordingly their role as a local
resource.]{.underline} Other climate change related phenomena included
unpredictable temperature fluctuation and heavier winds and rainfall[;
these may impact game mating and hunting times and alter fish migration
patterns. The overall consequences of this remain unclear. Arctic
communities are already burdened by significant disparities in food
security and health, and climate change enhances this through changes in
local food systems, food security and affordability.]{.underline}

###  inequality 

#### Climate change imposes [social]{.underline} and [financial strains]{.underline} on Indigenous communities via [shifts from traditional practices]{.underline} and [relocation]{.underline}\-\--significantly [constrains]{.underline} [adaptation]{.underline}.

**Malik**, Postdoctoral Research Fellow at the School of Geography,
University of Leeds, **and Ford**, Professor and Priestley Chair in
Climate Adaptation at the Priestley Centre for Climate Futures, **2025**

\[Ishfaq Hussain, James D, "Barriers and limits to adaptation in the

accessed 7.12.25, JCP-LL\]

[As climate change alters the Arctic environment]{.underline}, many
[Indigenous communities are forced to shift]{.underline} away
[from]{.underline} [traditional]{.underline} subsistence
[practices]{.underline}, [leading to increased dependence on expensive
technologies]{.underline} for food gath- ering, imported foods, and fuel
\[76\]. The reliance on technologies such as high-efficiency snowmobiles
and gasoline for transportation, advanced fishing equipment and boats
for food gathering and travelling, and GPS systems and satellite phones
for navigation has increased \[••20,23,84\]. However, [buying these
technologies is **costly**, imposing a significant **social** and
**financial strain** on communities and creating **social
inequities**]{.underline}. [This is exacerbated by an increased reliance
on expensive store- bought foods, a high cost of living, and dependence
on global markets and price fluctuations]{.underline} \[••65\].
[This]{.underline} eco- nomic shift [presents a **barrier** for
**lower-income house- holds** that cannot afford **adaptation
costs**]{.underline}, [as well as a **limit** for communities that have
surpassed economic feasibility thresholds.]{.underline}

[Inability to adapt due to financial constraints presents a significant
limit to adaptation]{.underline}. In Chukotka, Russia, many Indigenous
communities live in poverty, with limited access to resources and
infrastructure \[53\]. This [financial constraint creates a limit to
adaptation, as communities cannot afford the technologies or infra-
structure needed to cope with climate change.]{.underline} The [limited
mobility due to fixed settlements acts as a limit.]{.underline} [In
Alaska, many Indigenous communities were forcibly relocated to fixed
settlements]{.underline}. [These settlements are now vulnerable to
rising sea levels and permafrost thaw, limiting the ability of
communities to adapt through traditional mobility practices]{.underline}
\[25,51\].

### Loss of Culture -- general 

#### Arctic climate change unique -- and impacts indigenous livelihood and culture -- multiple warrants 

**Mardikian, a PhD in International Law and Galani, sits at the
Non-Executive Board of Advisors of Human Rights at Sea, 23** 

Lisa and Sofia, "Protecting the Arctic Indigenous Peoples' Livelihoods
in the Face of Climate Change: The Potential of Regional Human Rights

https://academic.oup.com/hrlr/article/23/3/ngad020/7237938, HDSCW
07-09-2025, IS

[Climate change has had a profound impact on the living environment of
Arctic indigenous peoples, and thus, it merits special attention. The
Artic is characterised 'as the world's climate change
barometer']{.underline}.25 According to the reports of the
Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC), '[surface warming in
the Arctic will continue to be more pronounced than the global average
warming over the 21st century']{.underline}.26 It is expected that
['additional warming will lead to more frequent and intense marine
heatwaves and is projected to further amplify permafrost thawing and
loss of seasonal snow cover, glaciers, land ice and Arctic
sea]{.underline} ice'.27 [A significant decline in sea ice and glaciers
is already being witnessed]{.underline} in the Chukchi and Beaufort
seas.28 [Decline in seasonal sea ice and higher sea levels increase
coastal hazards, such as flooding and coastal erosion]{.underline}.29
Such changes have resulted in, and are projected to further [lead to,
restrictions on indigenous peoples' access to ancestral lands and
natural resources, loss of land and houses or forced relocation and mass
resettlements, which directly affect the enjoyment of the right to
property]{.underline}.30 In addition, [there have been serious
consequences for the main sources of income of indigenous communities,
as the thinning of ice makes it more difficult to hunt and fish and
climate change has led to the degradation of reindeer pastures and to a
lack of available food for reindeer herding]{.underline}.31

[Ocean warming and acidification are also responsible for the depletion
of fish stocks and decline in other marine species, limiting the
opportunities of Arctic indigenous people to access marine resources,
threatening their income, food security and cultural
heritage]{.underline}.32 [Reduction in sea ice may facilitate
overfishing, which, in turn, is likely to affect the fish stocks and the
fishing practices of Arctic indigenous peoples, many of whom rely on
small-scale subsistence fishing]{.underline}.33 Sea-level rise can also
change coastal configuration or lead to the partial or complete loss of
islands. This will affect the baselines from which maritime zones are
measured and may result in their reduction or disappearance. 34 [Such
scenario could severely limit the access of the Arctic indigenous
communities to natural resources, including their fishing
rights. ]{.underline}

## Impact -- climate change bad -- resource wars 

### Internal link -- Arctic climate change competition 

#### Climate change increases competition in Arctic -- and impact indigenous people 

**Gendler,**visual writer for VOA Graphics focusing on geopolitics and
international news,**25**

\[Alex,"Why the Arctic matters in the 21st century",VOANEWS,January
31,2025,
http://voanews.com/a/why-the-arctic-matters-in-the-21st-century-/7958931.html
HSCW,ZT\]

[The Arctic is one of the coldest and least populated regions on Earth,
much of it covered by ice. But in recent years it has become one of the
most important sites of geopolitical tensions]{.underline} --- and a key
focus of American policy.Despite its inhospitality, land north of the
Arctic Circle has long been inhabited by Indigenous people like the
Inuit, Sami and Yukaghir and today includes territories belonging to
eight countries: Canada, Russia, Iceland, Finland, Norway, Sweden,
Denmark and the United States.In 1996, these countries formed the Arctic
Council --- a forum that includes all eight countries as member states
along with representatives from Indigenous groups. [But while the Arctic
was once envisioned as a neutral zone where research and conservation
could promote deeper international cooperation, multiple developments
since then have turned it into a site of competition]{.underline}.[The
number one issue facing the Arctic is climate change]{.underline}. Since
the 1990s, Arctic sea ice has declined by 7.6 trillion metric tons, with
the rate of loss increasing by 57%. [Besides contributing to rising sea
levels, the loss of ice also reduces global solar reflection. This
creates a feedback loop as the darker ocean water absorbs more heat,
causing more ice to melt, adversely affecting global weather
patterns]{.underline}.[The melting of Arctic ice also directly affects
local wildl]{.underline}ife, with polar bear populations projected to
decline by two-thirds in the next quarter-century as they lose their
hunting grounds.But where some see environmental disaster, others see
opportunity[. The melting ice is making Arctic trade routes more
navigable]{.underline}, providing shorter distances for transoceanic
shipping than current lanes using the Suez and Panama canals.
Furthermore, [increased navigability is expanding potential for
exploration and extraction of natural resources.The Arctic region is
estimated to hold over 20% of the world's remaining fossil fuel
reserves, with over 400 oil and gas fields already discovered.
Both]{.underline} [the seabed and offshore areas also hold vast
quantities of minerals]{.underline} ranging from staple commodities like
iron, gold, nickel and zinc to rare earth metals such as neodymium and
dysprosium, which are used in electronics and battery technology. [Even
traditional subsistence activity may be greatly altered and expanded as
global warming leads fish stocks to migrate north and more coastal land
becomes available for agriculture.\
Yet economic opportunities in the Arctic are emerging at a time of
increased geopolitical tensions, as countries scramble to secure
resources, stake territorial claims and develop
facilities]{.underline}.With 53% of the Arctic coastline under its
control, Russia has the largest presence in the region in terms of
civilian ports like Murmansk and Arkhangelsk as well as multiple
airfields and military bases along its northern border. More recently,
[Russia has moved to expand its claims to the Arctic seabed]{.underline}
at the same time that its invasion of Ukraine in 2022 has led other
members of the Arctic Council to suspend cooperation with Moscow.\
While land jurisdiction in the Arctic is largely settled aside from a
few small disputes, maritime claims are much more complex. Control over
Arctic waters is generally governed by the United Nations Convention on
the Law of the Sea (UNCLOS), which defines multiple types of territorial
waters where a nation may have the right to restrict the activity of
foreign vessels. [These range from internal waters that are considered
part of a nation's sovereign territory to exclusive economic
zones]{.underline} where foreign ships may travel freely but cannot
extract resources.Although the United States played a formative role in
negotiating the treaty and abides by most of its provisions, it remains
one of the few countries that has not formally acceded to it because of
concerns about limitations it places on seabed mining.Even among UNCLOS
signatories, however, boundary definitions can vary. While Canada
considers parts of the Northwest Passage to lie within its territorial
waters, for example, most other nations including the United States
consider it to be an international strait where foreign ships may
transit.Similarly, Russia has claimed parts of the Northeast Passage
along its northern coastline as internal waters, moving to restrict the
right of passage in areas where it was previously allowed.Given these
disputes, the Transpolar Sea Route through the center of the Arctic
Ocean, which lies fully in international waters, will become more
attractive as polar ice continues to thaw.\
[The increasing importance of the Arctic has attracted the attention of
other powers without Arctic territory.]{.underline} Several of these
states have been admitted as observers in the Arctic Council, including
Germany, the United Kingdom, Japan and South Korea. [China, which is
also an observer, has unilaterally declared itself as a "near-Arctic
state"]{.underline} and has expanded both research and commercial
activity in the region by partnering with Russia as well as investing in
infrastructure in Norway, Iceland and Greenland.\
[China's increased presence in the Arctic alongside Russia's more
aggressive posture]{.underline} have been among the reasons cited for
U.S. President Donald Trump's insistence on annexing or buying
Greenland, a territory of NATO ally Denmark. While Greenland already
contains a U.S. military base on its northwest coast, the discussion is
likely to result in a further militarization of the territory, even
under Denmark.[Satellites are also expected to play a major role in
exerting control within the Arctic,]{.underline} given the importance of
observation and monitoring in remote areas with poor communications
infrastructure.\
[What was once considered a frozen frontier with little to offer is
quickly becoming one of the most contested regions on Earth. And as the
planet heats up, competition in the Arctic will as well.]{.underline}

#### Climate change increase competition over Arctic 

[[[Şöhret]{.underline}]{.mark}](https://dergipark.org.tr/en/pub/@mesutsohret),
Doctor of philosophy, 25

\[[[[Mesut]{.underline}]{.mark}](https://dergipark.org.tr/en/pub/@mesutsohret),
"Geopolitical Impacts of Climate Change: Arctic Case", YÖNETİM VE
EKONOMİ, 03/12/25
[[https://dergipark.org.tr/en/download/article-file/4013136]{.underline}](https://dergipark.org.tr/en/download/article-file/4013136)
7/14/25 HDSCW-CP\]

[[Projected changes in temperature, precipitation, sea level, and
extreme events pose numerous risks to ecosystems and the people who
depend on them]{.underline}]{.mark}. Risks differ according to the
characteristics of social and ecological systems. Some ecosystems,
[[such as those in the Arctic]{.underline}]{.mark} and Antarctic, [[are
susceptible to changes in temperature]{.underline}]{.mark}. The [changes
that will occur in these regions]{.underline}, which are seen as the
world\'s refrigerators, [have the potential to cause geopolitical
changes and disrupt the world\'s climate structure.]{.underline} [With
the effects]{.underline} of global [climate change, the current and
potential value of new energy basins, and the opening of sea
passageways, the Arctic is turning into a region with a high potential
for conflict rather than the cooperation]{.underline} of regional and
non-regional actors and becoming one of the geopolitical centers of
gravity of the world. [The Arctic]{.underline} is a [region with unique
geographical and climatic conditions]{.underline}, [various natural
resources, and strategic importance]{.underline}. With the melting of
glaciers, [this area, which has increased international interest, is
becoming an important center for both scientific research and commercial
activities.]{.underline} In this respect, the Arctic is the northernmost
region in the world and has many special geographical, astronomical, and
climatic features. Accordingly, the Arctic extends from 66.5° North
Parallel, centering on the North Pole, to 90° North. The Arctic is
located in the northern part of Asia, Europe, and North America. It
surrounds the Arctic Ocean, which is an ocean covered with ice. It is
the largest body of water in the Arctic; it is the smallest and
shallowest ocean in the world. When evaluated according to its
geographical integrity, its social and cultural integrity, the land and
sea areas spread within the borders of the Arctic States. Of these, the
Arctic and open seas are exceptional areas, and there are also areas of
conflict and problems (Barents Sea, Lomonosov Ridge, Sea Passageways,
etc.). Although it is valid for the whole world\'s geography,
technological advances in the context of the Arctic increase the
importance of the field, even if it reduces its impact. [The Arctic acts
as a strategic bridge between North America and Eurasia]{.underline}.
This location facilitates access to sea routes and natural resources,
thus attracting the attention of great powers.

will emerge as a result of the melting of the glaciers causes the region
to be at the center of international politics due to its potential
economic and political effects. [The importance of the Arctic, which is
becoming increasingly accessible as the ice melts as a result of rising
global temperatures, should not be underestimate]{.underline}d. It seems
inevitable that the region will be at the center of activities such as
discovering gold and other precious metals, commercial shipping,
tourism, and oil and gas exploration due to its changing structure.
[Apart from the natural cycle and natural changes of the Earth, it is

impact on the circulation of surface water and sea ice in the Arctic

## AT climate offense 

### Securitization good 

#### Turn---climate securitization can be a useful tool for transformative democracy---stressing urgency is necessary

**Albert,** University of London, Lecturer in International Relations,
**2023**

(Michael, "Climate emergency and securitization politics: towards a
climate politics of the extraordinary," GLOBALIZATIONS, 2023, VOL. 20,
NO. 4, 533--547,
[[https://doi.org/10.1080/14747731.2022.2117501]{.underline}](https://doi.org/10.1080/14747731.2022.2117501)
HDCSW-TM)

Michael Williams suggests that [by rethinking securitization theory
through the lens of extraordi- nary politics, it is possible to develop
a more positive understanding of securitization as 'a process of
openness and self-determination with democratic potential']{.underline}
that may enable 'a more founda- tional -- if always fraught --
revaluation of the political order itself in ways that can be inclusive
and reformative as well as violently exclusionary' (2015, p. 115). In
other words, from this view, [there is no inevitability that emergency
claims (if accepted) lead to suppression of democracy and exclusionary
injustice]{.underline}, though this is always a danger. Instead,
[enunciations of security or declarations of emergency from grassroots
movements may be viewed as expressions of latent con- stituent
power]{.underline} that also have democratizing potential. As Ben
Anderson shows, [both climate and racial justice movements use emergency
declarations as 'a pragmatic-contextual intervention in the present that
aims precisely to disrupt what has already become normal',]{.underline}
or to fold 'a sense emer- gency into the mode of operation of the very
systems and infrastructures that, for some, once pro- duced a sense of
stability' (2017, p. 473, 472). In this way [they aim to generate
urgency among otherwise complacent publics, erode the legitimacy of
constituted political regimes, inspire hope that rapid transformative
action can stave off the emergency and create a better future, and call
forth 'self-organized insurgent publics' capable of realizing this
alternative future]{.underline} (Anderson, 2017, pp. 469--470; Kalyvas,
2008, p. 13).

[The strategy of Extinction Rebellion can usefully be read in this way.
Their key demands]{.underline} -- for- cing governments to 'tell the
truth' about the climate emergency, bring emissions to zero within a
decade, and create 'citizen's assemblies' to democratically determine
how societies decarbonize -- [aim to disrupt and transform dominant
cognitive--affective dispositions by highlighting the unsus- tainability
of business-as-usual, the criminal negligence of existing governments
vis-à-vis the cli- mate emergency, and the necessity of decisive
emergency action.]{.underline} Like earlier forms of civil disobedience,
their disruptive actions 'target directly the existing symbolic and
juridical structures of instituted power' in order to rearticulate the
'illegal' as 'extra-legal', or subject to a 'higher law' or rationality
beyond the arbitrariness of instituted state authority (Kalyvas, 2008,
p. 299). Further- more, by calling for citizen's assemblies they
explicitly aim to supplement institutionalized demo- cratic
decision-making with 'extraconstitutional forms of participation that
strive to narrow the distance between rulers and ruled, active and
passive citizens, representatives and represented' (7).

But of course there is no guarantee that grassroots climate emergency
strategies will result in the desired outcome: target audiences may
remain unpersuaded by the urgency of the crisis and/or the need for
emergency action beyond current policies, and dominant interests and
their media mouth- pieces may effectively write them off as
'eco-extremists' or 'millenarian cultists' (the common dis- cursive
strategy employed by rightwing UK media outlets). On the other hand, [in
conditions of worsening climate shocks and deepening political-economic
crises, climate emergency movements may indeed help trigger 'social
tipping points' in which the need for emergency action rapidly becomes
popular common sense]{.underline} (Otto et al., 2020), though the
outcome may be eco-authoritarian securitization more than climate
justice (as I'll elaborate below). [Critics of climate emergency strat-
egies]{.underline} are thus not wrong when they highlight the risks of
such strategies. They **[are simply one-sided, ignoring or downplaying
their democratic and constituent potential.]{.underline}**

## Artic key 

### Unique role 

#### Arctic Scholarship that engage indigeneity key to challenge the structures 

**Latola**, Research coordinator on Arctic issues at the Thule
Institute, University of Oulu, **2025**

\[Kirsi, "Engaging With Arctic Stakeholders and Rights Holders in
Research and Economic Development Projects," *The Routledge Handbook of
Arctic Governance*, 2025, https://doi.org/10.4324/9781003371885,
accessed 7.10.25, JCP-LL\]

A growing need for societally relevant research in [the
Arctic]{.underline} has risen as global attention has turned to the
Arctic, which [is warming]{.underline} nearly [four times faster than
any other region in the world2]{.underline}. [This causes harmful
effects on Arctic residents']{.underline} everyday [lives, cultures,
well-being, economies and infrastructures]{.underline}, just to mention
a few. As the slogan states, "[What happens in the Arctic, doesn't stay
in the Arctic]{.underline}", [the climate change in the Arctic creates
**global socio- economic impacts** and changes in processes which are
important for **global climate** and **sea level**]{.underline}3. [These
are]{.underline} most [strikingly witnessed as **extreme weather
events**]{.underline}. At the same time, the Arctic has become
attractive for new opportunities for economic growth, e.g., the use of
its natural resources and the building and investment in green energy
transition.

All [these]{.underline} [have increased the need for **stakeholder
dialogue**, **engagement**, or **consultations**,]{.underline} depending
on the context. There are over seven million people living in the Arctic
region, and approximately one million people, [9% of the total
population of the Arctic region, are Indigenous]{.underline},
representing over 40 different ethnic groups4. The United Nations
Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples (A/RES/61/295) adopted
on 13 September 2007, defines Indigenous rights as follows: 1.
Indigenous Peoples have the right to the lands, territories, and
resources which they have traditionally owned, occupied or otherwise
used or acquired. 2. Indigenous Peoples have the right to own, use,
develop and con- trol the lands, territories, and resources that they
possess by reason of traditional ownership or other traditional
occupation or use, as well as those which they have otherwise acquired5.
Therefore, in this chapter [Indigenous communities are defined as rights
holders]{.underline}.

The European Union (EU) identified the key societal challenges in its
H2020 framework programme, which was a starting point for extensive
stakeholder dialogue with the aim of conducting societ- ally relevant
research6. This was the basis for EU-PolarNet, an EU Coordination and
Support Action project funded for two periods (2015--2020 and
2020--2024). The Stakeholder Engagement Work Package 2 of the
EU-PolarNet project is the basis for this chapter. Table 11.1 provides a
summary of EU-PolarNet project activities conducted by the Stakeholder
Engagement Work Package.

In particular, this chapter discusses Arctic stakeholder and rights
holder engagement in research and economic development projects.
Indigenous communities are acknowledged as rights holders and because
best practices and guidelines for ethically sound research created by
several Northern communities including the Inuit Circumpolar Council,
The Saami Council and the Alaska Native Knowledge Network, among others,
are available online, this chapter is not duplicating their effort but
acknowledges them as best resources and sources of knowledge. In
addition to this, useful open resources with a compilation of resources,
recommendations, and best practices from a variety of Northern
organizations can be found on the Arctic Research of the United States
(ARCUS) web- site. A "Roadmap to decolonial Arctic research" has been
recently released: It is a comprehensive policy brief communicated to
the EU Commission and contains 32 recommendations for actions and best
practice examples with the aim to anchor decolonial research in the
Arctic. This was co-created by the Saami Council together with several
partners, and the process also included two dialogues with the European
Commission7. This Roadmap provides the needed guidance for Arctic
researchers and funders.

How to Engage With Stakeholders and Rights Holders in Research

[Arctic research has expanded]{.underline} during the recent years
resulting in a 22% increase in Arctic research publications during
2016-- 2021 8. [This has increased requests and needs for stakeholder
and rights holder engagement and dialogue with the aim to, first,
**fulfil funding agencies\' criteria** for **societally relevant
research** and, sec- ondly, to move towards **co-produced
transdisciplinary research**]{.underline}. [By engaging in these
activities, research can be more **representative** of stakeholder's
interests and knowledges, and more **impacting** in terms of academic
records]{.underline}9.

[Non-academic participants can bring multiple **knowledges** and
**expertise**, which are **vital** to research and give **flexibility**
on the idea of how research should be framed, conducted and become
successful]{.underline}10. But [this entails, creating an environment of
**joint leadership**, **shared rights** and **obligations** from the
onset of the project]{.underline}11. [Research expectations must be
agreed upon and managed through the project's lifetime in an on-going
open and respectful dialogue.]{.underline} [This is vital for building
**trust**]{.underline} when crossing professional cultural boundaries,
[because this entails stakeholders and rights holders opening them-
selves to vulnerability and risks]{.underline}. Harris and Lyon12 write
that "trust is shown to be built by having information on others, prior
experience of working together, norms of cooperation, and sanctions
exerted on those who might transgress norms of behaviour".

## Government policy key 

#### Indigenous representation [combats Euro-western epistemological policy making process]{.underline} -- integration is the only way to stop the sidelining of native voices in the USFG. 

**Maddox, PhD, and Ninomiya, PhD, 2024**

\[Raglan and Morton, "Indigenous sovereignty in research and epistemic
justice: Truth telling through research", Global Public Health, December
11, 2024, https://doi.org/10.1080/17441692.2024.2436436 accessed July 1,
2025 HDCSW - TW\]

[**Our sovereignty is embodied, it is ontological** (our being) **and
epistemological** (our way of knowing), and it is grounded in complex
relations derived from the inter-substantiation of ancestral beings,
humans and land. In this sense, our **sovereignty is carried by the body
and differs from western constructions of sovereignty**, which are
predicated on the social contract model, the idea of a unified supreme
authority, territorial integrity and individual rights.]{.underline}
(Moreton-Robinson, Citation2007, p. 2)

[**Settler colonialism is rooted in white supremacy and aims to '"tame"
a variety of wildernesses**, end up establishing independent nations,
effectively repress, co-opt, and extinguish Indigenous alterities, and
\[productively\] manage ethnic diversity']{.underline} (Veracini,
Citation2011). Colonialism involves one nation subjugating another,
controlling and exploiting its people and resources. [Colonisers
commonly **force their own language(s), culture, values and beliefs on
the people** they control and actively **exclude Indigenous peoples from
academic institutions** and effectively attempt to **sideline Indigenous
knowledges**]{.underline}. By 1914, most of the world\'s nations had
been colonised by Europeans at some point, using power and influence to
control (Abernethy, Citation2000; Lloyd & Metzer, Citation2013;
Wesseling, Citation2015). Non-white and non-Euro-western knowledges have
been subsequently positioned as inferior and 'othered' within the
colonial racialised hierarchical ways of knowing and knowledges
(Valverde, Citation2005). [When Euro-western values and practices are
baked into colonial laws, regulations, political decisions and
institutional governance systems, people who do not conform to the same
values and practices are often discriminated against and
excluded.]{.underline} Such everyday racism and segregation of people by
race and culture often go unnoticed and unmentioned by groups who are
not subject to racism, such is the [**pervasiveness of whiteness** and
the role it plays in **determining what knowledges and practices are
valued**]{.underline} (Maddox, Kennedy, Waa, et al., Citation2022;
Robertson, Citation2015; Ward et al., Citation2021). The colonial
hierarchy of knowledge systems has resulted in daily experiences of
wide-ranging structural and systemic forms of discrimination, racism and
exclusion through colonisation and racialised capitalism (Anderson et
al., Citation2016; Gracey & King, Citation2009; King et al.,
Citation2009; Morton Ninomiya et al., Citation2023). While this
commentary may resonate with diverse geographies, colonial histories,
power and control differentials, we recognise and respect the great
diversity among Indigenous peoples, including diversity in experiences
of colonisation and coloniality.

Our commentary [calls for epistemic justice. **By epistemic justice, we
refer to how epistemologies** (how knowledge(s) is/are constructed) that
are **frequently devalued or ignored must be treated, recognised and
valued** -- with careful consideration of identity and power within
their contexts. We draw attention to how systems privilege some
knowledges while **silencing, delegitimising and devaluing differing
knowers** and ways of knowing, being and doing]{.underline} (Fricker,
Citation2017). Before discussing epistemic justice, we see value in
truth telling. This paper is structured to name and highlight examples
of truths that are commonly hidden in plain sight, predominately to
people in power, and then draw attention to epistemic justice in public
health research and practice globally.

Truth telling

We highlight two truths that are commonly hidden in plain sight. First,
[knowledge systems that drive educational systems, research, practices,
decisions and outcomes are **predominantly Euro-western oriented**. As a
result, they are systematically and actively biased, reinforcing
white-dominant perspectives through **inherently racist epistemological
processes** of whitewashing -- the deliberate or unintentional erasure,
modification or minimisation of non-Euro-Western perspectives,
contributions and knowledges. This process is harmful because it
**systematically devalues ways of knowing**, actively silences voices
and reinforces dominant narratives that privilege Euro-Western
views.]{.underline} [The **result is a distortion of history,
knowledges** and research impacts decision-making and outcomes,
particularly for Indigenous and other structurally and systemically
oppressed peoples and communities]{.underline} (Lee et al.,
Citation2023). Second, [scientific research and researchers tout using
principles of openness, collaboration and transparency and yet continue
to draw on inherently racist epistemological processes and racialised
logic that inherently perpetuate and embed racism, inherently silencing
and undermining Indigenous knowledge systems and knowledges]{.underline}
(Miller, Citation2022). While our commentary is framed around
sovereignty amid structural and systemic racism, colonisation and the
exclusion of Indigenous peoples, we know that much of our position also
applies to hetero- and gender-normative, ableist, ageist and a plethora
of interlocking systems of power, control and oppression. The following
words from Dr Rhys Jones' at the 2022 PRIDOC conference resonate with
us: '[We cannot pick our oppressor. **We must be antiracist, antisexist,
antiablist, antiageist, antioppresive**]{.underline} ... '. Examples of
how knowledge systems are Euro-western oriented and whitewashed can be
found in questioning whose knowledge(s) are (de)valued and numerous
related questions (Box 2). We suggest truth telling includes drawing
attention to answers from the following questions.

#### Recognition of indigenous knowledge systems key to challenging colonial knowledge systems 

**Maddox, PhD, and Ninomiya, PhD, 2024**

\[Raglan and Morton, "Indigenous sovereignty in research and epistemic
justice: Truth telling through research", Global Public Health, December
11, 2024, https://doi.org/10.1080/17441692.2024.2436436 accessed July 1,
2025 HDCSW - TW\]

[[Indigenous ways of knowing, being and doing are based on embodied
sovereignty, relationality and countless generations of knowledge
sharing.]{.mark} We **[call for epistemic justice in which Indigenous
knowledge systems are recognised and valued]{.mark}**]{.underline} in
research-related contexts. We draw attention to how [[colonial knowledge
systems **silence, delegitimise and devalue specific knowers and ways of
knowing, being and doing** -- through truth
telling]{.mark}.]{.underline} This includes (1) the extent to which
educational systems, research, practices, decisions, and reported
outcomes are whitewashed -- [a process of structural and systemic
discrimination, racism, and exclusion]{.underline} that actively alters
or omits Indigenous and non-Euro-Western contributions and perspectives
to fit Euro-Western norms and (2) whitewashed and racialised logic in
scientific research [that]{.underline} claims to be open, collaborative
and transparent. Whitewashing not only [[obscures the history and
contributions of Indigenous peoples and communities but also actively
reinforces systemic biases and inequities.]{.mark} We assert the need
for epistemic justice in public health research. [**Epistemic justice
calls for Indigenous sovereignty and self-determination** to be made
visible. It may involve on how colonial policies, protocols, and
regulations are connected to everyday lived inequities of Indigenous
communities, families and individuals. Ultimately, epistemic justice is
inherent to Indigenous peoples' health and wellness, self-determination
and sovereignty]{.mark}.]{.underline}

Colonial context: Epistemic justice

Indigenous peoples are diverse and are made up of many nations,
languages, political systems, knowledges, experiences, practices,
relationships and [[myriad forms of resistance to
colonisation]{.underline}]{.mark}. [These relationships commonly include
connections to the land, waters, air, ice and traditional medicines,
such as the sacred tobacco plant. [**Indigenous ways of knowing, being
and doing have existed for thousands of years**.]{.mark}]{.underline}
[[They are based on sovereignty and countless generations of
observations, experiences, growing knowledges and sharing
information]{.mark} (Box 1). [These knowledges are intimately connected
to relationality]{.mark}. It is important to recognise relationality and
our credentials. These are founded in our respective relational roles,
community accountability and responsibilities as well as acknowledging
our connections, biases and worldviews. [Relationality is a distinct
Indigenous social research presupposition and forms the epistemic
'scaffolding' shaping and supporting the possibility for coming to know
and generating knowledge(s) in respective times, spaces and
places]{.mark} (Moreton-Robinson, Citation2017, p. 69).]{.underline} The
successes of careful and valuable research, evaluation and knowledge
generation are evidenced through survival and adaptions to everchanging
environments, including colonialism, programmes and policies embedded to
actively eradicate Indigenous people and communities, as well as the
active silencing and attempted control around relationality.

#### The aff's recognition of indigenous knowledge related to development ensures research that shifts the gaze from Indigenous peoples to colonial systems 

**Maddox, PhD, and Ninomiya, PhD, 2024**

\[Raglan and Morton, "Indigenous sovereignty in research and epistemic
justice: Truth telling through research", Global Public Health, December
11, 2024, https://doi.org/10.1080/17441692.2024.2436436 accessed July 1,
2025 HDCSW - TW\]

Epistemic justice in public health research

Settler colonisation, which inherently includes Indigenous-specific
racism, is well established as a root cause of negative health and
wellbeing within Indigenous populations (Greenwood et al., Citation2015;
King et al., Citation2009, p. 1--2; Paradies, Citation2016). **[In
communities where Indigenous sovereignty and self-determination are
realised, so too is health and wellbeing]{.underline}** (Joseph,
Citation2024). The UNDRIP (United Nations General Assembly,
Citation2007), Uluru Statement from the Heart (Referendum Council,
Citation2017), Truth and Reconciliation Commission (Citation2015), Close
the Gap (Department of the Prime Minister and Cabinet, Citation2020),
Reconciliation Australia (Reconciliation Australia, Citationn.d.),
national research councils (Government of Canada, Citation2014; National
Health and Medical Research Council (NHMRC), Citation2023) and local
councils assert that improving Indigenous health and wellbeing outcomes
is everyone's business. In other words, [if researchers are not
improving Indigenous health and wellbeing outcomes through research and
evaluation, chances are that they will exacerbate
inequities]{.underline}. [When [']{.mark}one-size-fits-all' research,
policies and protocols are applied, systemic and structural inequities
are unaccounted for and often exacerbate health inequities]{.underline}
(Delaney et al., Citation2021). **[Researchers and evaluators must
consider the health and wellbeing needs of Indigenous peoples, and the
contexts in which they operate.]{.underline}**

**[Epistemic justice requires honouring Indigenous peoples' sovereignty
and self-determination]{.underline}**

[Epistemic justice will improve Indigenous health and wellbeing
outcomes]{.underline}. In fact, [epistemic justice has improved
Indigenous health and wellbeing outcomes]{.underline}. There is an
ever-growing body of research and practice demonstrating epistemic
justice in Indigenous public health research and practice, as is
featured in this special issue. In a current realist review study
(Hunter Porter et al., Citation2024) [focussed on Indigenous knowledges
centred in research, we are finding that few publications report on the
(1) extent to which Indigenous community members are involved in all
phases of research, (2) research outcomes and benefits for the
community, (3) involvement of community members in writing the paper and
(4) relationship of the authors to the Indigenous
community(ies).]{.underline}

[It is possible that more Indigenous communities will engage in research
that 'studies up' -- research that shifts the gaze from Indigenous
peoples to the contemporary colonial structures and systems that support
and maintain the conditions for health and wellbeing inequities and
inequalities]{.underline}. Researchers and scholars have drawn attention
to examples and the need for more empirical research that makes visible
the insidious nature of current structural racism and its direct impacts
on health (Jongbloed et al., Citation2023; Ray et al., Citation2022;
Wispelwey et al., Citation2023). Research methods like institutional
ethnography have shown how polices, protocols, regulations, processes
and environments are directly linked to peoples' daily activities,
including driving health inequities and inequalities (Balcom et al.,
Citation2021; Morton Ninomiya et al., Citation2018). By studying
colonial systems and institutions in relation to the everyday lived
experiences of Indigenous peoples and communities, researchers may be
required to 'bite the hand that feeds them' to address inequities and
inequalities, upholding ethical practice. Studying up requires research
applicants and funders alike to ask questions such as: what will it take
for funding bodies to support this kind of research? Does this research
come at an expense we are willing to make? Who is willing to take the
risk of biting the hand that feeds their programme of research? Imagine
studying the protocols and practices of organisations/institutions that
fund research with Indigenous communities and groups and journals that
publish research papers on Indigenous research. We need to consider who
is most appropriate and qualified to adjudicate proposals and papers?

Championing antiracist work across society, [within the academy, and
throughout health systems requires including and respecting Indigenous
knowledges, cultures and ways of knowing, being and doing. In return,
health and wellbeing outcomes will undoubtedly improve among Indigenous
peoples]{.underline} and non-Indigenous peoples. We need to better
understand and make visible the connections and complexities within
systems, rather than accept or adopt simple cause-and-effect approaches
and explanations. [It is important that colonial systems recognise and
authentically respect Indigenous values, knowledges and cultures, using
an actively antiracist approach]{.underline}. [Understanding the ongoing
impacts of colonisation and racist policies, including truth telling,
can help improve policy, practice and ultimately health and wellbeing
outcomes. By practicing and championing epistemic justice, researchers,
health practitioners and systems at large can help to reverse centuries
of exclusion, racism, inequities and inequalities that Indigenous
peoples continue to face today.]{.underline}

## Politics of survivance 

### Indigenous agency -- survivance politics 

Indigenous

Jakobsen et al, Associate Professor at University of Copenhagen, 24

\[Uffe, "Introduction: indigenous self-governance in the Arctic States",
The Polar Journal, 28 May 2024,
[[https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/2154896X.2024.2342116]{.underline}](https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/2154896X.2024.2342116),
access date: 7/13/25, HDCSW SZ\]

[A key point in this literature is that]{.underline} [political autonomy
can take many different forms.]{.underline} At the one extreme, it can
be derivative from the constitutional structures of the state of which
it is part such as unitary and federal states. [At the other extreme,
the autonomy can be non-territorially based, and thus not tied to the
state structures in a simple way. This can be due to the dispersion of
the Indigenous populations concerned on the territory of the
state.]{.underline} Hybrids between the two, or political autonomy which
is only partially derivative of state structures, are also possible. In
addition, [the institutional outcomes are also shaped by whether
self-government arrangements are a response to aspirations for future
independence or rather to Indigenous demands for more control in
particular (functional) areas. Second, there is the literature on
Indigenous political governance. The focus here is on how Indigenous
people are enacting political agency rather than being passive receivers
of political decisions from the dominant state elites.]{.underline}

This literature sees [the interaction between Indigenous
peoples]{.underline} and the states as dynamic and ongoing; Indigenous
people [are not on verge of disappearing from the political stage but
rather engaged in a politics of 'Survivance' (native presence and
actuality), which should be recognised within political
science.]{.underline} Moreover, [although Indigenous people seek
equality, their aims, unlike other ethnic groups, often include some
kind of 'sovereign' government within their existing. Even if the
empowerment of Indigenous peoples through the UNDRIP and ILO conventions
is important, the concrete establishment of Indigenous autonomy comes
out of the inter-action within the states structures of which they are
part.]{.underline} [An]{.underline} HI ([historical
institutionalism]{.underline}) [framework provides concepts to address
the importance of this context, central historical events, goal-seeking
political actors and their identity.]{.underline} [In addition, the
application of HI allows an integration of the agency of Indigenous
peoples into theoretical approaches where this has previously been
absent.]{.underline}

### Development = governance

#### Development is peacebuilding, governance, human rights, and humanitarian assistance. 

**MFAJ, Ministry of Foreign Affairs of Japan, 15**

Ministry of Foreign Affairs of Japan 15. "Cabinet decision on the
Development Cooperation Charter". 
[[https://worldjpn.net/documents/texts/docs/20150210.O1E.html]{.underline}](https://worldjpn.net/documents/texts/docs/20150210.O1E.html)
HDCSW-Packet

For the purpose of this Charter, the term \"development cooperation\"
refers to \"international cooperation activities that are conducted by
the government and its affiliated agencies for the main purpose of
development in developing regions.\" In this connection,
\"**[development]{.underline}**\" in this Charter is [used in a
broader]{.underline} [sense]{.underline} rather than in the narrow
sense; it also [encompasses]{.underline} such activities as
**[peacebuilding]{.underline}** and **[governance]{.underline}**,
promotion of basic **[human rights]{.underline}** and **[humanitarian
assistance]{.underline}**.

### Arctic -- fluid definition 

#### There isn't one coherent definition of the Arctic -- political, cultural, and climate considerations all impact the definition -- means you can't vote on what is and isn't 

**Arctic Center @ The University of Lapland, no date**

The University of Lapland's Arctic Center, "Arctic Region",
[[https://www.arcticcentre.org/EN/arcticregion]{.underline}](https://www.arcticcentre.org/EN/arcticregion)
HDCSW-TM\]

[The Arctic region, or the Arctic, **is a geographic region spreading
around the North Pole. There is no single correct definition of the
region as the southern boundary varies.**]{.underline}

Key ways to define the Arctic:

The Arctic Circle (66 ° 33\'N) delimits the Arctic in terms of solar
radiation. In theory, areas north of the Arctic Circle have at least one
day without daylight in the winter and at least one nightless night in
the summer. In practice, this does not happen everywhere because the
surface of the earth is uneven, and the light refracts in the
atmosphere.

Based on temperature, the monthly average temperature in the Arctic is
below + 10 ° C throughout the year, even in summer.

The forest line follows a temperature-defined area. The forest line is
not a narrow line but a zone tens of kilometres wide between the
northern coniferous forest and the tundra. In this demarcation, the
Arctic is predominantly wooded tundra and glaciers.

Permafrost increases the area of Russian Arctic compared to the other
delimitations. Permafrost is soil that stays frozen for at least two
consecutive years.

[The ice cover determines the Arctic nature of marine areas. Sea ice is
highest in February-March and lowest in September. The surface of the
Arctic ice is monitored almost in real time by satellites. (see The
National Snow and Ice Data Centre)]{.underline}

[Culturally defined, the Arctic covers the homelands of northern
indigenous peoples.]{.underline}

[Political delimitations vary according to how they serve, for example,
the interests of states or international cooperation.]{.underline}

[As the climate warms, **the Arctic shrinks if defined by temperature,
forest line, permafrost, or ice cover.** **Cultural and political
boundaries also vary.** The Arctic Circle is the most permanent of the
delimitations, although also the polar circle moves very slowly due to
the variation of the Earth\'s axial tilt.]{.underline}

**Oxford 19** English Dictionary Online, "it's",
https://www.oxfordlearnersdictionaries.com/definition/english/its

[belonging to or connected with a thing, an animal or a
baby]{.underline}

Turn the box on its side.

Have you any idea of its value?

The dog had hurt its paw.

The baby threw its food on the floor.
