### Advantage Counterplans 

#### Proposals that don't attend to micropolitics fail in government policy bad 

\*AT: Advantage Counterplans that don't directly engage Indigenous
peoples

**Lezak**, Scott Polar Research Institute, University of Cambridge,
**and Rock**, Resident at Native Village of Shaktoolik, **2024**

\[Stephen, Genevieve, "On Micropolitics: Climate adaptation and
Indigenous governance in Western Alaska," Climatic Change 177, 135
(2024). https://doi.org/10.1007/s10584-024-03769-4, HDCSW-KGrid
7/10/25\]

3.2 Analysis: identifying micropolitics

[In]{.underline} the [existing]{.underline} academic and government
[literature]{.underline}, [commonly proposed interventions]{.underline}
[to improve]{.underline} the delivery of [environmental
assistance]{.underline} are well-informed and urgent. But these
recommendations sometimes **[fall short]{.underline}** [of engaging the
more mundane and micropolitical barriers to effective collaboration with
Indigenous communities]{.underline}, such as Alaska Native villages.
[Issues that seem distant from federal offices]{.underline} in
Washington, DC, [can pose significant challenges in rural
Alaska.]{.underline} These include, for example, intermittent Internet
access, English spoken as a second language, misaligned expectations
around trust and relationship-building (Whyte 2020), and the need to
spend days to weeks at a time away from village centers to fish and hunt
(Marino 2015). [**Effectively assisting** with environmental
adaptation---and indeed, any issue---in rural Alaska requires attending
to these **place-based** and **culturally-specific dimensions**, and the
**micropolitics** that arise between them]{.underline}.

A list of micropolitical barriers, with examples and exceptions, is
presented in Table 1. Neither the list, nor the exceptions, are
exhaustive. Table 2 presents a list of non-micropolitical barriers ---
also non-exhaustively.

[Alaska Native villages face micropolitical barriers to successfully
engaging federal programs even when those programs]{.underline} (e.g.,
those within the Bureau of Indian Affairs) [are specifically designed to
render aid to Indigenous communities.]{.underline} Often, [these
barriers are technological, cultural, or pragmatic in ways that are not
legible]{.underline} (Scott 2008) [to agency staff in cities in the
Lower 48]{.underline}. [These barriers are micropolitical because they
are **not readily visible** to policymakers but nevertheless pose
**major challenges** in the practice of policy execution and service
delivery.]{.underline}

In this case, [micropolitics is about the conduct of government rather
than its overarching goals]{.underline}. As such, [they are easily
overlooked or]{.underline} even [ignored]{.underline} by high-level
officials in Congress and the agencies tasked with delivering services.
Nevertheless, [they underlie the conditions for success or failure in
many communities, as was the case following Typhoon Merbok.]{.underline}

# DA answers 

## K of the DA 

#### Discourses of extinction like your DA impact universalize obscuring the logics of white supremacy 

**Mitchell,** Canada Research Chair in Global Political Ecology at the
Balsillie School of International Affairs, **and Chaudhury,** PhD
student at the Department of Science and Technology Studies, York
University, **2020**

(Audra and Aadita, "Worlding beyond 'the' 'end' of 'the world': white
apocalyptic visions and BIPOC futurisms," International Relations 2020,
Vol. 34(3) 309--332, https://doi.org/10.1177/0047117820948936, accessed
HDCSW-TM)

**[[Discourses that predict the]{.mark} imminent '[end of the world' are
not]{.mark} as [universal]{.mark} as they often claim to
be.]{.underline}** [[The futures they fear for]{.mark}, seek to protect
[and]{.mark} work to [construct are rooted]{.mark} in]{.underline} a
particular set of global social structures and subjectivities:
[[whiteness]{.mark}. Whiteness]{.underline} is not reducible to skin
pigmentation, genetics or genealogy. It [is a set of cultural,
political, economic, normative, and subjective structures derived from
Euro- centric societies and propagated through global formations such as
colonization and capitalism. These [multi-scalar structures work by
segregating bodies through the inscription of racial difference,]{.mark}
privileging those they recognize or construct as 'white'4 and unequally
distributing harms to those that they do not.]{.underline}5 Whiteness is
also a form of property6 that accrues benefits -- including material,
physical, and other forms of secu- rity -- and pervasive forms of power,
across space, time, and social structures. Due in part to its
trans-formation through long-duration, global patterns of violence and
con- quest, [whiteness takes unique forms wherever and whenever it
coalesces, so it should not be treated as universal -- despite its own
internal claims to this status. Most of the leading contributors to
mainstream ['end of the world' discourses]{.mark} discussed in this
arti- cle [are rooted in Euro-American cultural contexts]{.mark}, and in
particular in settler colonial and/or imperial states]{.underline} such
as the United States, Canada, Australia, and the United Kingdom. As
such, **[the forms of whiteness they embody are [linked to]{.mark}
particular histo- ries of [settlement]{.mark}, frontier cultures,
resource-based [imperialisms, genocides of Indigenous communities,
histories of slavery, and modes of
anti-Blackness.]{.mark}]{.underline}**

Whiteness is remarkable in its ability to render itself invisible to
those who possess and benefit from it. [Many, if not most, of the (often
liberal humanitarian) authors of 'end of the world' discourses seem
unaware of its integral influence on their thinking]{.underline}, and
would almost certainly be horrified at the thought of their work
entrenching racialized injustices. We are not suggesting that these
authors espouse explicit, intentional and/or extreme rac- ist ideals, on
which much public discussion by white people of racism tends to focus.7
Nor do we wish to homogenize or present as equivalent all of the
viewpoints discussed in this paper, which display a range of expressions
of whiteness and levels of awareness thereof.8 On the contrary, [we work
to center broad, everyday, structural ways in which underlying logics of
[whiteness and white supremacy frame and permeate mainstream paradigms
and discourses, including those identified as liberal, humanitarian, or
progressive]{.mark}]{.underline}. Even amongst white people who
consciously and explicitly disavow racism, [unconscious, habitual,
normalized, structurally-embedded assumptions circulate, and are
reproduced in ways that perpetuate race9 as a global power
structure.]{.underline} This includes one of the authors of this paper
(Mitchell), who, as a white settler,10 continues to benefit from and
participate -- and thus 'invest'11 -- in structures of whiteness, and
therefore has a continual responsibil- ity to confront them (although
total divestment is not possible).12

The 'habits' of racism13 are reflected strongly in the way that
contemporary 'end of the world' narratives frame their protagonists:
those attributed with meaningful agency and ethical status in the face
of global threats; those whose survival or flourishing is prioritized or
treated as a bottom line when tradeoffs are imagined and planned; and,
crucially, those deemed capable of and entitled to 'save the world' and
determine its future. This is expressed in several key features of the
genre, including its domination by white thinkers; the forms of
subjectivity and agency it embraces; and the ways it con- trasts its
subjects against BIPOC communities.

First, [contributors to fast-growing fields like the study of
'existential risk' or 'global catastrophic risk' are overwhelmingly
white]{.underline}. As we will see, almost all of the authors identified
by the literature review on which this paper is based, and certainly the
most influential thinkers in the field, are white. For example, [[the
seminal collection Global Existential]{.mark} Risk]{.underline},14 which
claims to offer a comprehensive snapshot of this field, [is [edited by
two white male Europeans]{.mark} (Nick Bostrom and Milan Circovic) [and
authored by an almost entirely white (and all-male) group of
scholars]{.mark}]{.underline}. Likewise, [the most senior positions
within influential think tanks promoting the study of 'existential
risk', such as the Future of Humanity Institute, the Cambridge Center
for the Study of Existential Risk and Humanprogress.org, are dominated
by white men, with few exceptions]{.underline}.15 Another expression of
this tendency toward epistemic whiteness is found in the habit,
prominent amongst white academics, of citing all or mostly-white
scholars, which entrenches a politics of citation16 that privileges
whiteness and acknowledges only some intersectionalities as relevant.17
As mentioned above, Mitchell's (2017)18 work offers an example of this
tendency: while it engages critical, feminist, and queer post-
apocalyptic visions written by white authors, it does not center BIPOC
perspectives or knowledge systems.

These examples do not simply raise issues of numerical representation,
nor can white- ness necessarily be dismantled simply by altering these
ratios. More importantly, [[all-white or majority white spaces]{.mark}
create epistemes in which most contributors share cultural back-
grounds, assumptions, and biases that [are rarely challenged by
alternative worldviews, knowledge systems or registers of
experience]{.mark}]{.underline}. In such epistemes the perceived
boundaries of 'human thought' are often elided with those of
Euro-centric knowledge. For example, influential American settler
journalist David Wallace-Wells19 contends that there exists no framework
for grasping climate change besides 'mythology and theology'. In so
doing, he ignores centuries of ongoing, systematic observation and
explicit articulations of concern by BIPOC knowledge keepers about
climactic change. [[The bracketing of BIPOC knowl- edges]{.mark} not
only severely limits the rigor of discourses on global crises, but also,
as bi-racial organizer and thinker adrienne maree brown20 argues, **it
[produces distorted outcomes]{.mark}**. For instance, it smuggles
normative judgments that 'turn Brown bombers into terrorists and white
bombers into mentally ill victims' into apparently 'objective'
claims]{.underline}. Similarly, the influential work of Black American
criminologist Ruth Wilson Gilmore21 demonstrates how white imaginaries
of the threat posed by BIPOC bodies has produced the massive global
penal complex and the radically unequal distribution of life chances. In
short, **[[imagi- naries create worlds, so it matters greatly whose are
privileged, and whose are excluded.]{.underline}]{.mark}**

Further, [emerging narratives of the 'end of the world' explicitly
center figures of whiteness as their protagonists -- as the survivors of
apocalypse, the subjects capable of saving the world from it, and as
those most threatened]{.underline}. In these discourses, ['survivors'
are framed as saviors able to protect and/or regenerate and even improve
Western forms of governance and social order by leveraging resilience,
scientific prowess, and techno- logical genius.]{.underline} For
example, the cover of American settler scientists Tony Barnosky and
Elizabeth Hadley's book Tipping Points for Planet Earth features a
stylized male 'human' whom they identify as former California governor
Jerry Brown (a powerful white settler politician) holding the earth back
from rolling over a cliff.22 Similarly, presenting a thought experiment
about the planet's future, Homer-Dixon23 asks his readers to imagine 'an
average male -- call him John' (in fact, the most popular male name
globally at the time of writing was Mohammed). This is followed by
images of a Caucasian male dressed in safari or hiking gear -- both
emblematic of symbols colonial conquest24 -- tasked with choosing from
two forks on a path, as imagined by white American poet Robert Frost.
This image of rugged masculine whiteness, embodied in physical strength,
colonial prowess, and the ability to dominate difficult landscapes is
mirrored in his framing of his former co-workers on oil rigs in the
Canadian prairies25 as models of resilience. Similarly, American settler
science writer Annalee Newitz26 proposes the Canadian province of
Saskatchewan as a 'model for human survival', based on her perceptions
of the resilience, persistence and collaborative frontier atti- tudes of
its people. Saskatchewan is a notoriously racist part of Canada, in
which vio- lence against Indigenous people continues to be integral to
its white-dominated culture27 -- yet this polity and its culture are
held up by Newitz as a model of 'human' resilience. [By imagining
subjects in whom whiteness is elided with resilience and survival,
[these discourses not only normalize and obscure the modes of violence
and oppression through which perceived 'resilience']{.mark} -- or, in
blunt terms, preferential access to survival -- [is achieved. They also
work to displace the threat of total destruction 'onto others who are
seen as lacking the resourcefulness of the
survivor']{.mark}]{.underline}.28

## Permutations/engagement with law possible 

### Perm -- two-eyed seeing 

#### [Perm Two-Eyed Seeing]{.underline} -- do the aff as a starting point for the alternative \-- bringing together Western and Indigenous legal orders for best of both worlds -- utilizes legal pluralism to promote Indigenous sovereignty and fine-tuned solutions -- solves better than the alternative 

**Young**, Assistant Professor, University of Ottawa, Common Law
Faculty, **2023**

\[Frankie, "Etuaptmumk: A Means to Advance Indigenous Economic
Development "in a Good Way"," Dalhousie Law Journal, Volume 46, Issue 1,
2023,
https://digitalcommons.schulichlaw.dal.ca/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=2215&context=dlj#:\~:text=Etuaptmumk%20or%20Two%2DEyed%20Seeing,or%20transdisciplinary%20or%20collaborative%20work.,
HDCSW -- TM \]

2\. The Mi'kmaw principle of Etuaptmumk (Two-Eyed Seeing) as a means to
appeal to the best of both Western laws and Indigenous laws in economic
development

Historically, [the privileging of [Western laws in Indigenous]{.mark}
economic [development]{.mark}]{.underline}[, [has had deleterious
impacts]{.underline}]{.mark} [on Indigenous economies because Indigenous
laws have gone **unrecognized**.]{.underline} Yet [[bringing together
the **best of both Western and Indigenous legal orders**]{.mark} in
economic development processes [could]{.mark} be a means to [provide a
more **equitable** basis]{.mark} from which [to promote Indigenous
**self-determination**, **community** **engagement**,
**empowerment**,]{.mark} and [**capacity** **building**]{.mark},85 [and
respect for Indigenous **sovereignty**]{.mark}.]{.underline} [The
[Mi'kmaw principle]{.mark} of Etuaptmumk (**[Two- Eyed Seeing]{.mark}**)
is a useful principle that [encourages **diverse ways** of knowing to
work together]{.mark}.]{.underline} It is based on the premise that [to
"see from one eye with the strengths of Indigenous knowledges and ways
of knowing, and from the other eye with the strengths of Western
knowledges...and \[learning to use\] both these eyes together, for the
benefit of all]{.underline}"86 [is [a more **equitable** way to view and
experience shared]{.mark} [spaces]{.mark}.]{.underline} Indeed, [the
existence of two separate social systems]{.underline} (based in values,
legal orders, and economic theories) [means Two-Eyed Seeing can be
explored, discussed, and positioned as a **reconciliation framework**
for more **ethical** or **just** legal and economic
development.]{.underline}

Elder Marshall notes that [the principle can apply in a range of
circumstances and in many areas of society]{.underline}:

[Two-Eyed Seeing]{.underline}...does not fit into any particular subject
area or discipline. Rather, it [is about life]{.underline}: [what you
do, what kind of responsibilities you have, how you should live while on
earth]{.underline}...[i.e., a guiding principle that covers all aspects
of our lives: social, economic, environmental, etc.]{.underline} **[[The
advantage of Two-Eyed Seeing is that you are always fine tuning your
mind into different places at once]{.mark}, you are [always looking for
another perspective and better way of doing
things]{.mark}]{.underline}**.87

While Two-Eyed Seeing is helpful beyond the law, this article focuses on
the law to challenge legal actors to embrace the strengths of Indigenous
laws and ways of knowing in combination with the strengths of Western
laws and ways of knowing. As such, [[Two-Eyed Seeing can
expand]{.underline}]{.mark} Canadian [[legal orders to recognize and
legitimize Indigenous legal orders]{.mark}.]{.underline} Moreover, [what
"Two-Eyed Seeing" can bring to the "economic table" is that Indigenous
legal orders [not only]{.mark} have the capacity to [shape **legal
pluralism**]{.mark}]{.underline} in Canada [[but]{.mark} can [shape
Indigenous]{.mark} economic [development]{.mark} in a way [that supports
Indigenous self-determination.]{.mark}]{.underline} To do this, [an
**interdependent relationship** must be established where parties are
willing to engage in a co-learning journey.]{.underline}88 This is
distinguished from the ways that Indigenous laws have been treated as
inferior to Canadian laws, rather than as laws that all parties can
learn from on a co-learning journey between Indigenous and
non-Indigenous legal actors.

[[Engaging with different legal perspectives]{.mark}---which contain
both similarities and differences---[is a more inclusive way to
formulate complete legal principles in a given
area]{.mark}.]{.underline} For instance, Two-Eyed Seeing can be useful
to promote diversity in the area of trust law. Indigenous trusts have
become an increasingly common means for Indigenous communities to
advance economic development. Under Canadian law, trustees have a
fiduciary duty to act in the best interests of the beneficiaries in
carrying out their duties in relation to the assets of the trust.
Likewise, Indigenous peoples across Canadaincluding the Nisga'a,
Gitxsan and Gitanyow, Cree, Anishinaabe, Nlaka'Pamux, Kwanlin Dün, and
Mi'kmaqhold distinct legal orders that set out the multi-faceted
responsibilities and obligations for managing wealth and resources.89

### Epistemic delinking 

#### Epistemic de-linking key to challenge binary of coloniality/modernity to combat epistemic marginalization -- deconstruction of IPR racial logics opens spaces for new emancipatory knowledge 

**Vats**, Associate Professor of Law at the University of Pittsburgh
School of Law, **2020**

\[Anjali, "Conclusion," [The Color of Creatorship]{.underline}, Stanford
University Press, 2020, DOI:
https://doi-org.dartmouth.idm.oclc.org/10.1515/9781503610965, HDCSW-TM\]

[Decolonial theory, like CRT, foregrounds the need to make intellectual
property's racial politics visible and confronts the exclusionary
effects of in- clusionary politics]{.underline}, through histories of
modernity as well as the histories of Americanism and citizenship
highlighted in this book. For decolonial theorists such as Walter
Mignolo and Anibal Quijano, [the colonial is always already in
opposition with modernity, with the latter category associated with
universal norms of "development" and "progress."]{.underline}

[[Decolonial thought]{.mark}, accordingly, [breaks with]{.mark} the
fundamental ["myth of modernity," embracing]{.mark} instead practices of
["epistemic delinking"" from the binary of coloniality/modernity created
by the political]{.mark} and economic systems]{.underline} that
originated in Europe and spread to the colonies. More spe- cifically,
["\[d\]e-coloniality turns the plate around and shifts the ethics and
politics of knowledge.]{.underline} Critical theories emerge from the
ruins of languages, categories of thoughts and subjectivities (Arab,
Aymara, Hindi, French and English Creole in the Caribbean, Afrikaans,
etc.) that had been consistently negated by the rhetoric of modernity
and in the imperial implementation of the logic of coloniality."8
[Through this emphasis on centering marginalized categories of thought,
[decoloniality provides a]{.mark} useful [theoretical frame for thinking
across racial and national identity categories,]{.mark} in ways that aid
in [undoing anti-Blackness, anti-Indigeneity, and anti-Asianness, among
oth- ers]{.mark}.]{.underline} As José David Saldivar explains,
[[decolonial theory]{.mark} is a [useful]{.mark} tool [for thinking the
politics of "becoming minor," and]{.mark} the [epistemic marginal-
ization that accompanies them]{.mark}]{.underline}.

### Perm - Aff solve starting point 

\*\*note this also answers cap

#### Spirit of co-existence key and analysis must begin from the starting point of Indigenous peoples position today

**Young**, Assistant Professor, University of Ottawa, Common Law
Faculty, **2023**

\[Frankie, "Etuaptmumk: A Means to Advance Indigenous Economic
Development "in a Good Way"," Dalhousie Law Journal, Volume 46, Issue 1,
2023,
https://digitalcommons.schulichlaw.dal.ca/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=2215&context=dlj#:\~:text=Etuaptmumk%20or%20Two%2DEyed%20Seeing,or%20transdisciplinary%20or%20collaborative%20work.,
HDCSW -- TM \]

Nonetheless, Borrows notes that "despite recognition of the plurality of
legal orders in Canada, there is a paucity of research on the ways in
which various Indigenous legal orders intersect with fiduciary
duties."90 The result is that rather than learning from Indigenous
interpretations of law and managing resources, many trustees end up
equating the fiduciary duty with a responsibility to focus primarily on
fiscal returns. [This is not to say that the idea of profit is somehow
"outside" or anathema to Indigenous economic principles, but rather that
profit should not be the sole focus to the exclusion of other
values.]{.underline}

For example, [one of the legal principles that guide the Mi'kmaq is
Netukulimk, which confers the people with a responsibility to
**respect** and **steward** the **land** and **resources** with future
generations in mind.]{.underline} [The use of all that Creator has
provided is intended to support the economic well-being of individuals
and the community without endangering the integrity, diversity, or
productivity of the environment.]{.underline} [Profit should not be the
sole focus because the responsibility to use resources in a sustainable
way involves spiritual and traditional elements, as well as reciprocity,
all intended to connect the people, the flora, the fauna, and the
environment.]{.underline}91 Just as Western laws are established to
promote economic development, [Netukulimk promotes and sets the
**ecological limits** of economic development]{.underline}. In terms of
managing trust assets, combining the best of both Western and Indigenous
legal orders is a more holistic way to advance Indigenous economic
development in a good way.92

Certainly, [dealing with multiple legal orders within one broader area
of law will not be a simple or easy process.]{.underline} [In
applying]{.underline} what Elder Marshall believes is **[the spirit of
co-existence]{.underline}**,93 [a weaving back and forth between Western
laws and Indigenous laws in economic development ventures may be
required in some instances.]{.underline} To this end, [in advocating for
Indigenous legal engagement in economic development initiatives and
processes, it is clear many of these initiatives involve a market-based
accumulation of assets.]{.underline} [While there is no denying the
devastating impacts of capitalism, the **starting point** should be
where **Indigenous** **peoples** are positioned **today** and not where
they were before capitalism became pervasive.]{.underline} The reality
is that [many communities are engaging in the market
economy.]{.underline} Accordingly, [the question should be about how to
engage in a manner that supports Indigenous sovereignty and
prosperity.]{.underline} [It is necessary to reimagine how Indigenous
peoples' economic development opportunities---largely disrupted by
Western legal systems that solely focused on recognizing non-Indigenous
interests at the expense of Indigenous interests---can be informed by
Indigenous legal orders.]{.underline}

## Cap k 

### Turn/we solve the alt better 

#### Indigenous practices traditionally better for socio-ecological sustainability to challenge extractivism -- bolstering indigenous culture key

**Dunlap**, visiting research fellow at the University of Helsinki,
**2023**

\[Alexander, "Chapter 3 The Structures of Conquest: Debating
Extractivism(s), Infrastructures and Environmental Justice for Advancing
Post-development Pathways," [The Afterlives of Extraction: Alternatives
and Sustainable Futures, 2023,
https://doi.org/10.1163/9789004686182_004, HDCSW-TM\]]{.underline}

Establishing renewability is challenging, but it remains a remedy to
extractivism and ecological catastrophe. Various [[**Indigenous**
**traditions**]{.mark} [and **practices**]{.mark} that]{.underline},
before the onset of industrialisation (Deloria, 1999; Hatfield et al.,
2018; Ulloa, 2020), [cohabited and continue to this day to cohabit with
the planet [are]{.mark} certainly [examples of **(real) socio-ecological
sustainability and renewability**]{.mark}]{.underline} or, at least, are
closer to it. [[(Neo)colonialism, modernity and
capitalism]{.underline}]{.mark}, and their products, [[are
spreading]{.mark} everywhere---[creating **ruptures** with renewable
socio-ecological relationships]{.mark}.]{.underline} Winona LaDuke and
Deborah Cowen (2020, 253) remind us that [human activity should be
'rooted in a practice of relationality that understands human and
more-than-human life as kin, as familial relations']{.underline} (see
also Simpson, 2014). [[Acknowledging]{.underline}]{.mark}, [respecting
and living with [non-human life]{.mark}]{.underline} (e.g. animals,
trees, rivers, mountains), [[and challenging anthropocentrism]{.mark}
[and materialism]{.mark}]{.underline} (in the mechanical philosophy
sense), [offers important pathways to [challenge
**extractivism**]{.mark}]{.underline}---[[as countless **Indigenous land
defenders**]{.mark} [do]{.mark} across the world]{.underline} (Blaser,
2013; Escobar, 2020; Springer et al., 2021)---[[and]{.mark} to
[begin]{.mark} conceptualising **[real socioecological
renewability]{.mark}**.]{.underline} This means remembering, as Leanne
B. Simpson (2014, 9--10) explains, that [humans are 'dependent upon
intimate relationships of reciprocity, humility, honesty and respect
with all elements of creation, including plants and
animals']{.underline} (see also Escobar, 2020). **[Extractivism denies
this reality]{.underline}**, [instead viewing everything as a 'resource'
and a commodity to be taken, marketed, and sold]{.underline}
(Grosfoguel, 2016; Simpson, 2019). This also relates to the 'predator
prey bargain', popularised by Derrick Jensen (2006, 179),6 which
conceptualises, based on Indigenous practices, our responsibility with
regard to consumption and, by extension, 'resource use'.

If you live on a piece of land-if you own a piece of land-if you consume
the flesh that is on that land, you are now responsible for the
continuation of that land and its health. You are now responsible for
the health of all the various communities who share that land with you.
And because members of this community will consume your flesh, too, they
will be just as responsible for the continuation and health of your
community. At that point you will own the land, and it will own you.

jensen, 2006, 197

This conceptualises how to organise systems based on care and
renewability, as opposed to plunder and extractivism. Moreover, it is a
reminder that [we share our habitats with other peoples]{.underline},
which also stresses that [if people damage or destroy their habitats,
they are destroying themselves.]{.underline} The anarchist slogan,
'destroy what destroys you', obtains greater significance from this
perspective. The carelessness of and the destruction wrought by
modernist development have gone on so long that they are resulting in
climate change and weather extremes (Hickel, 2020). This understanding,
then, adds to our conceptualisation of renewability as well as of
extractivism. [Extractivism is thus defined as cutting, fracturing,
taking, usurping and disregarding humans and non-humans without
intention or plan to fully and adequately redress the resulting violence
and harm.]{.underline}

### Perm/legal engagement good 

#### Incorporating indigenous legal analysis into critique of Western law breaks down negative externalities of capitalism.

Perm/link turn v cap k

**Young**, Assistant Professor, University of Ottawa, Common Law
Faculty, **2023**

\[Frankie, "Etuaptmumk: A Means to Advance Indigenous Economic
Development "in a Good Way"," Dalhousie Law Journal, Volume 46, Issue 1,
2023,
https://digitalcommons.schulichlaw.dal.ca/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=2215&context=dlj#:\~:text=Etuaptmumk%20or%20Two%2DEyed%20Seeing,or%20transdisciplinary%20or%20collaborative%20work.,
HDCSW -- TM \]

Similarly, Anderson et al assert that Indigenous economic participation
should be grounded in traditional values. They advocate for the
principles of social entrepreneurship, which align economic engagement
with a defined social purpose. Social entrepreneurship takes into
account certain socially defined limits on economic activity. As wealth
increases and communities are able to provide targeted access to
education, housing, social programs, and health care, to name a few, the
well-being of community members also increases.58 Through social
entrepreneurship, Indigenous peoples can benefit from long-term economic
growth, not as an end in itself, but rather as a means to promote the
control of traditional lands and local activities.59 While this is an
effective means for Indigenous entrepreneurs, [models of social
entrepreneurship developed by non-Indigenous peoples do not consider
**Indigenous sovereignty** and the role of **Indigenous legal
traditions** and values in shaping business, including in social
enterprises.]{.underline}

Each of the above authors make a valuable contribution to the
scholarship on Indigenous economic development. In expanding upon these
scholarly contributions, this article is concerned with the role law has
played, and can play, in shaping Indigenous economic development. [Given
the impact law has had in advancing capitalism and adversely impacting
Indigenous sovereignty, one cannot consider solutions exclusive of the
law.]{.underline} [It is not enough to say that capitalism and
Indigenous economic values diverge, or that the core tenets of
capitalism are so dominant that they will lessen the value of Indigenous
traditions.]{.underline}60 Rather, [**examining** the legal system that
was, and remains, instrumental in advancing capitalism may assist in
**dismantling** its effects.]{.underline} [This requires a **reckoning**
with how one economic system became the most pervasive and dominant
system for conducting business and engaging in economic activity in the
Western world.]{.underline}

Arguably, [just as strengthening Western law played a critical role in
advancing capitalism, **strengthening Indigenous law** can play a
profoundly critical role in advancing **Indigenous sovereignty**,
**self-determination**, and **prosperity**.]{.underline} [One cannot so
easily get away from the way law materially shapes the lives of
individuals in the particular societies in which they live.]{.underline}
[Western law has been, and is, a powerful accomplice in the
dispossession of Indigenous sovereignty and lands which has resulted in
substantial economic inequity]{.underline}. [However, reclaiming and
incorporating Indigenous laws, rooted in Indigenous knowledge and social
systems, into Indigenous economic development initiatives and processes
is a way to counter these effects.]{.underline}

While Newhouse did not specifically implicate the law, he alluded to the
ways that **[capitalism could be countered by incorporating Indigenous
values in economic development.]{.underline}** Likewise, Atleo concedes
that [a resurgence of **Indigenous** **knowledges**, traditions,
culture, and values may **counter the impacts of capitalism** on
Indigenous economic development]{.underline}. [However, there needs to
be something more.]{.underline} That "something more" is the way in
which law is understood in Canada. Because Indigenous laws have largely
been ignored and, more importantly, overruled by Western law within the
greater Canadian legal system, their authority has been significantly
eroded.61 The ways Western law has been applied to determine the
significant legal, economic, political, and social outcomes of
Indigenous peoples should no longer be reinforced by the Canadian state.
Arguably, [seeking solutions solely through the same system of law that
disrupted Indigenous sovereignty, and, in turn, economic prosperity, is
an exercise in futility.]{.underline}

[It is in **redefining legal identity** that the law has the potential
to have an emancipatory effect for Indigenous peoples, and a positive
impact on Indigenous prosperity.]{.underline} Specifically, the function
of Indigenous legal orders can lead to this emancipatory effect. That
is, the solution lies in Indigenous peoples' meaningful contribution to
shaping legal pluralism in Canada, and in this instance, how Indigenous
law can shape economic development. Indigenous legal traditions have a
long history in Canada and many communities have developed complex
systems of law based on traditional knowledge and stories. As discussed
in the following section, by respecting the principle of Two-Eyed
Seeing, these laws can not only be recognized within the Canadian legal
system but legally validated in the same manner as the common and civil
law systems.

### Turn -- black indigenism 

#### Our engagement of Black-indigenism forms new sites of resistance against anti-black colonialism 

\*\*name check fugitivity/marroonage as site of shared resistance means
we access the alt

**Limes-Taylor Henderson**, University of North Georgia professor,
**2019**

\[Kelly, ""I had never been at home in the world"1: A case for
Black-Indigenism," *Curriculum Inquiry*, Volume 49, 2019 - Issue 1:
Sylvia Wynter, the Human, and Curriculum Studies,
https://doi.org/10.1080/03626784.2018.1556562, HDCSW-TM\]

Looking, Going Back

[[Black-Indigenism gives name to and/or subsumes particular types of
de-/anti-colonial thought and praxis that have long been in existence,
namely, de-/anti-colonial thought and praxes that prioritize the
experiences and liberation of both Black and Indigenous peoples]{.mark}
worldwide]{.underline}. In the Black American context, a protectionist
or nation-bound experience of Blackness often obscures Black American
connection to and involvement with historic intra- and international
liberation movements (Clemons & Jones, 2001, p. 20). Despite this lack
of acknowledgement, [the numerous examples of Black American engagement
with diasporic or other-worldly liberatory work hints at the pos-
sibilities promised within the wider Black-Indigenist
paradigm.]{.underline}

Drake (1993) notes that [Haitian independence became a symbolic of the
global Diasporic Black imaginary as a "'real' history" that was
"available for inspiration instead of mythic history"]{.underline} (p.
452). Further, from the time of Haitian independence [throughout the
1800 s,]{.underline} [Black American emigrationists offered departure
from the United States as the primary solution to the ills of anti-Black
settler colonialism]{.underline}, while W.E.B. DuBois, Anna Julia
Cooper, and Marcus Garvey (who was active both in his home country of
Jamaica as well as the United States) all famously addressed the plights
of African- descended peoples around the world (Clemons & Jones, 2001,
p. 20). [The 1950s-1960s independence movements of sub-Saharan African
countries were compelling to Black movements worldwide, and Black
American civil rights leaders' and organizations' later opposition to
the Vietnam War was well-known and based on implications that the war
was part of the larger and historical Western imperialist and
colonialist project.]{.underline} And, while [the Black Panther Party's
work]{.underline} and thought were heavily influenced by those resisting
imperialist and colonialist domination in their own contexts (such as
Fidel Castro, Che Guevara, Frantz Fanon, and Kwame Nkrumah), the Party
also [heavily influenced the work and thought of groups resisting
colonialist and racist contexts around the globe, including groups in
Israel, India, England, Australia, Bermuda, and the Bahamas]{.underline}
(Clemons & Jones, 2001; McCartney, 2001).

[These examples point to a Pan-Africanism orientation to
Black-Indigenist decolonizing movements.]{.underline} Drake (1993)
defined "traditional Pan-African activity" as "movement ... within
various parts of the \[B\]lack world to increase cultural contacts
between its diverse segments and to unite them in pursuit of common
interests," while defining the Black world as including "all those areas
where the population is actually \[B\]lack in a pheno- typic sense ...
or where the people think of themselves as \[B\]lack despite
\[additional non-African ancestry\] or where they are so defined by
others" (p. 453). In addition to this, cultural Pan-Africanism
highlights cultural ties between Black Diasporic and African peoples,
and does not necessarily have political aims (Drake, 1993, p. 463).
Drake notes that the most nuanced traditional Pan-Africanist political
thought and action connects global Black struggle to the struggles of
all oppressed peoples in the Global South -- and working-class peoples
around the world, whether in rural or urban environments -- while not
allowing Black interests to irretrievably disappear into those
struggles. Further, such nuance demands that Black people should
"maintain a high level of race con- sciousness in their local
situations" (Drake, 1993, p. 463).

While Pan-Africanist thought and work is reflected in the priorities of
Black- Indigenism, [[Black-Indigenism contends that the concept of
indigeneity is equally as important as that of Blackness,]{.mark} as
both concepts serve related and principal functions vis-\`a-vis Western
colonialism.]{.underline} [[Within Black-Indigenism, **liberation for
Black people about the world cannot be responsibly obtained without the
liberation of Indigenous people as well, and vice
versa**]{.underline}.]{.mark} Because of this, while Pan-Africanist
thought provides important supports for Black-Indigenism, it is not
complete, in that it primarily attends to the struggles and intelligence
of one (albeit massive) group of Indigenous peoples -- Black Africans --
rather than to those of Indigenous peoples the world over. In other
words, [Black-Indigenism equally attends to the struggles of Diasporic
Blacks]{.underline} (as the group that has been historically forced to
lose their indigeneity) [and the struggles of global Indigenous
peoples]{.underline} (as the group that has historically struggled to
maintain their indigeneity and/or Indigenous lands) [in an effort to
resist and heal from the effects of centuries of Western colonialist
dominance.]{.underline}

[With this Black-Indigenist dual prioritization in mind and within the
US context]{.underline}, it is important to note that documentation of
direct connections between Native and Black American resistance and
resilience work is not quite as pervasive as discussions of
Black-American Pan-Africanist work. Examples do exist, however. Jack
Forbes (1993) notes that, [in addition to researcher assertions of
Indigenous African and American maritime travel to each other's
respective continents before Columbus' landing, the "greatest degree of
intense contact between Americans and Africans did not occur ini- tially
in either the Americas or Africa, but rather in European
cities]{.underline} such as Lisbon, Seville and Valencia" [due to
Europe's trade in both Indigenous Americans and Africans during and
after the time of Columbus' genocidal exploits]{.underline} (p. 26). The
Iberian Peninsula had a long history of enslavement, with enslaved
peoples of "all colors and of many nationalities" becoming part of the
regional socioeconomic fabric throughout the fif- teenth, sixteenth, and
seventeenth centuries (Forbes, 1993, p. 27). While most of the enslaved
peoples were historically of European and North African descent,
increasing incursions into the Americas and sub-Saharan Africa brought
increasingly larger num- bers Indigenous peoples from both of these
continents together in Spain and Portugal.

As it became more economically expedient to keep Indigenous Americans
within the Americas, and as the plantation empires overtook the search
for precious metals, more and more Indigenous Americans were shipped
throughout the American contin- ent, while more and more Indigenous
Africans were kidnapped and brought to the Americas. Despite this
dominant trend, there is still evidence of Indigenous Americans' being
brought to Europe at the same time Indigenous Africans were, and even
evi- dence of Indigenous Americans' being sent to Africa -- whether as
enslaved or free workers, or for punishment (Forbes, 1993, pp. 37--39).4

While it can be easily assumed that such an extensive history of shared
enslave- ment encouraged the comingling of two groups who had never
previously encoun- tered each other in such numbers[, [this
history]{.underline}]{.mark} [often [encouraged]{.mark} [shared
resistance]{.mark} as well]{.underline}. [Perhaps the best long-term
example of this [shared resistance is in marronage and resistance
against enslavement]{.mark}, the phenomenon that took place across the
Americas as an often joint Indigenous American and
African/African-descended response to enslavement.]{.underline}
Beginning with the account of enslaved Africans and African-descended
people that were abandoned by or overtook their captors to escape "into
the interior of the US," Douglas (2015) goes on to explain Indigenous
American support of and/or subsequent community-making with formerly
enslaved African/African-descended people over 300 years ... and
European fear of such connections.

Early on in his book Robinson (1997) notes that, [with the "enactment of
the slave codes, both Black and Native American slaves were denied
allies in the Euro-American poor"]{.underline} (p. 9), indicating that,
[until 1776, slave revolts could have included enslaved Indigenous
Americans as well]{.underline} -- or enslaved people of both American
and African descent -- rather than Indigenous Americans' solely fighting
as allies of enslaved African-descended peoples. Further, Robinson
(1997) cites Aptheker (1939), who asserted that there was evidence of at
least 50 maroon communities in the southern United States (dated from
1672 to 1864), and that, contrary to what even the few his- torians of
marronage assert, "marronage ... was not just a Black phenomenon":
indeed, maroon communities not only included formerly enslaved
Indigenous Americans, or Indigenous Americans from "'destroyed
nations,'" but even the descents of escaped European indentured servants
and other poor Whites "'for whom there was no place in plantation
society'" (Learning cited in Robinson, 1997, p. 13). In fact, the very
first maroon communities in the English colonies were established by
European indentured servants, with the practice being overtaken by
non-European- descended peoples as Indigenous American genocide and
African enslavement expanded. For nearly 200 years, then, mixed-race,
predominantly Indigenous and Black American free communities existed
within the present-day states of Virginia, North Carolina, South
Carolina, Georgia, Florida, Alabama, Mississippi, and Louisiana at the
same time that the dominant sociopolitical systems of this region were
primarily based on racial segregation, enslavement, and Indigenous
genocide. Virginia's Dismal Swamp, Florida's Gracia Real de Santa Teresa
de Mose (likely an inspiration and a draw for enslaved people even
before Florida was taken by the United States), and many, many other
maroon communities throughout the Americas stood as testaments to the
fact that another world was possible -- a world in which the most
oppressed were able to conduct their lives in peace, safety, and
community without the constant threats of bondage or obliteration.

The above examples show that such [[collaborations repeatedly resulted
in sustained resistance to and survival despite dominant settler
colonial repression within the so- called United
States.]{.underline}]{.mark} These examples show us that **[[the concept
of Black-Indigenism is not only not new, but has a rich, extensive
history of collaborations between Indigenous Americans and
Africans/African-descended peoples toward survival under
settler-colonial power]{.mark}.]{.underline}** [While there are
unfortunate US examples of Indigenous American enslavement of Blacks;
the ostracizing of peoples of mixed Indigenous and Black American
descent; and of Indigenous and Black American mistreatment, violence,
polic- ing, and capture of each other in service to the settler colonial
project, it is important to view these examples within the context of a
society that has required violence against and the enslavement and death
of American Indigenous and African/African-descended people from its
beginnings.]{.underline} It is likely impossible for all people to
resist at all times, par- ticularly when their survival or safety
depends on their not resisting, or when a key strategy of the dominant
society is to pit groups against each other.

**Lezak**, Scott Polar Research Institute, University of Cambridge,

\[Stephen, Genevieve, "On Micropolitics: Climate adaptation and

1 Introduction

Rural [[**environmental adaptation** poses urgent challenges for
policymakers and societies]{.underline}]{.mark}. The World Bank
estimates that as many as 216 million people in vulnerable regions will
be internally displaced by climate-related events by 2050 (Clement et
al. 2021). The [scale and urgency]{.underline} of this multi-faceted
problem [[require aligning governments]{.underline}]{.mark} and NGOs
[[to support community-scale adaptation]{.mark} while minimizing
suffering and loss of autonomy.]{.underline} [[This is particularly true
for **Indigenous communities**]{.underline}, [whose ongoing battles for
**self-determination** are **doubly threatened**, as both environmental
change and adaptation]{.underline}]{.mark} (e.g., forced relocation)
[[can **impinge** upon **sovereignty**.]{.underline}]{.mark}

[[Climate change]{.underline}]{.mark}, with its dynamic hazard landscape
and shifting baselines, [p[oses]{.mark}]{.underline} a unique set of
[[challenges for disaster response and hazard
mitigation]{.underline}]{.mark} (Wilby and Keenan 2012). Many
well-intentioned rural climate adaptation efforts fall short of their
goals, often due to a combination of poor program design, ineffective
goal-setting, and fraught power dynamics (Pennington 2023). As a result,
[many governments and institutions are developing novel legal,
bureaucratic, and technical frameworks for adaptation and
resilience]{.underline} for threatened communities. The past decade has
seen a proliferation of such strategies and policies to improve
environmental adaptation and reduce maladaptation (Barnett and O'Neill
2010; Ulibarri et al. 2022). These new tools target diverse social and
political landscapes, from small island nations (Mcleod et al. 2019) to
large cities (Anguelovski et al. 2014). Given the breadth of human
societies and the myriad manifestations of environmental change,
strategies that work well in one context are not necessarily well-suited
to another.

This paper uses the concept of [**[micropolitics]{.underline}** to [draw
attention to a category of **procedural failures** in environmental
adaptation that are not policy failures per se, but **failures** in the
**implementation** of these policies, particularly when deployed in
cross-cultural or colonial settings.]{.underline} [These
failures]{.underline}]{.mark} often [[arise in mundane
issues]{.underline}]{.mark} that are missed in the high-level design of
the policies in question. They are adjacent to, but separate from,
issues of policy, goodwill, resource scarcity, or technical possibility.
[[Micropolitics are not micro because they are small or unimportant, but
rather because they are **fine-grained** and **easily
overlooked**.]{.underline} [Resolving micropolitical issues requires
**improved attention to the lived experiences** of threatened
communities and the dynamics that govern their interactions with
settler]{.underline}]{.mark} [[governments]{.underline}.]{.mark}

In the specific context of environmental adaptation[, [micropolitical
issues include]{.underline}]{.mark} (but are hardly limited to[)
[substandard communication between communities and
governments]{.underline}]{.mark} (Marino 2015); [[failure to incorporate
systems of traditional knowledge]{.underline}]{.mark} and governance
[[into adaptation plans]{.underline}]{.mark} (Raymond-Yakoubian et al.
2017); [[setting unrealistic expectations for small communities'
technical capacity]{.underline}]{.mark} (Minnes and Vodden 2017);
[failure to build trust]{.underline} (Whyte 2020); [[and simple
ignorance of historical contexts that necessitate an intersectional
approach to adaptation planning]{.underline}]{.mark} (Marino 2012;
Marter-Kenyon 2020; Whyte 2018).

[Arctic, northernmost region of Earth, centred on the North Pole and
characterized by distinctively polar conditions of climate, plant and
animal life, and other physical features.]{.underline} The term is
derived from the Greek arktos ("bear"), referring to the northern
constellation of the Bear[. It has sometimes been used to designate the
area within the Arctic Circle---a mathematical line that is drawn at
latitude 66°30′ N, marking the southern limit of the zone in which there
is at least one annual period of 24 hours during which the sun does not
set and one during which it does not rise.]{.underline} This line,
however, is without value as a geographic boundary, since it is not
keyed to the nature of the terrain.

### .1nc -- renewable link 

#### Renewable development is a spatio-temporal fix that shifts the crises of global capitalism to the global south. Their energy transition is built of exploitation and environmental devastation in the periphery.

**Saito 20** (Kohei, associate professor of political economy at Osaka
University, "Marx's Theory of Metabolism in the Age of Global Ecological
Crisis." Historical Materialism, Vol. 28, Issue 2, p. 3-24, HDCSW-TM\]

[The metabolic rift deepens with the development of
capitalism.]{.underline} In many cases, [it manifests itself as the
exhaustion of natural resources]{.underline}, their increase in price
and the corresponding fall of the rate of profit. Thus, [it
is]{.underline} quite [essential for capital to secure access to cheap
resources, energy and food. This is what leads capital to construct 'a
system of general exploitation of the natural and human
qualities]{.underline}' and 'a system of general utility' as Marx argued
in the Grundrisse:

> Hence exploration of all of nature in order to discover new, useful
> qualities in things; universal exchange of the products of all alien
> climates and lands; new (artificial) preparation of natural objects,
> by which they are given new use values. The exploration of the earth
> in all directions, to discover new things of use as well as new useful
> qualities of the old; such as new qualities of them as raw materials
> etc.34

This exploration of the earth and the [invention of **new
[technologies]{.mark}**]{.underline} by capital, however, [[**do not
repair the rift.** The rift remains **'irreparable' in capitalism.**
There is]{.mark} ultimately [the need to 'shift' the metabolic
rift]{.mark} to [somewhere else]{.mark}, not only [for the sake of
buying time]{.mark} but [also for minimising the manifestation of
contradictions in the centre.]{.mark}]{.underline}35 Here, corresponding
to the three dimensions of metabolic rift, there are also three ways of
shifting it.

First of all, [the metabolic rift manifested as the exhaustion of
natural resources, like soil exhaustion. Although]{.underline} Liebig
warned against the collapse of European civilisation due to robbery
agriculture, his prediction did not come true. This was because Fritz
Haber and Carl Bosch invented the so-called Haber--Bosch process in 1906
that enabled the industrial production of ammonia, and thus of chemical
fertiliser.

Nevertheless, the Haber--Bosch process did not heal the rift. The
production of ammonia (NH3) uses a massive amount of natural gas as a
source of hydrogen (H). It simply uses another limited resource to
produce ammonia, but it is quite energy intensive (it uses 2% of total
energy consumption) and produces a lot of CO2 (1% of the total carbon
emission). Furthermore, excessive usage of chemical fertiliser causes
eutrophication, as nitrogen and phosphorus, as well as water pollution,
as a result of their leaking into the environment. Soil ecology is
disrupted by the chemical fertiliser, the water-retaining capacity of
the soil goes down, and plants become more susceptible to disease.
Consequently, more and more fertiliser becomes necessary, as well as
agrochemicals and pesticide. These chemicals also pollute the
environment and disrupt the normal functioning of the ecosystem,
deepening the ecological crisis.

In short, [metabolic **[shift creates externality with]{.mark} the aid
of [new technologies]{.mark}**[:]{.mark} Soil fertility is artificially
maintained and even strengthened, while capital does not pay for
disruption, pollution and destruction in the wider
eco-systems.]{.underline} At the same time, [capital finds new business
opportunities in these disruptions, taking the opportunity to sell more
chemical fertiliser, agrochemicals and pesticide. This is how the formal
and real subsumption of nature under capital proceeds through the
metabolic shift.]{.underline}36

Secondly, there is spatial shift of the metabolic rift. Again, Marx
discussed this issue in relation to soil exhaustion. On the coast of
Peru there were small islands consisting of the excrement of seabirds
that had accumulated over many years. These islands were called guano
islands. Guano is quite rich in minerals that are useful for inducing
plant growth. 'Guano' originally meaning agricultural fertiliser in the
Andean Indigenous language Quechua, the Indigenous people traditionally
used it as dung. It was Alexander von Humboldt who encountered the
Indigenous usage of guano during his research trip to Peru in 1802. He
investigated the effectiveness of guano and tested it in European soils.
The result was positive, and its usage became quite popular across those
areas of Europe where soil exhaustion was a major social issue.

Soil exhaustion was a manifestation of the metabolic rift, but the
'solution' of guano did not repair the rift, as it simply shifted the
problem to the Global South. As a result, guano was continuously
transported from the periphery to the centre of capitalism, which
sustained soil fertility in Europe and the USA, thus providing food for
urban workers. On the other hand, its externality emerged in the form of
brutal oppression of Indigenous people, severe exploitation of thousands
of Chinese coolies, the rapid exhaustion of guano stocks, and more
general environmental degradation. Ultimately, the exhaustion of guano
reserves led to the Guano War (1865--6) and the Saltpeter War
(1879--84). In short, the antagonism between town and country within one
nation was spatially shifted and expanded to the global rift through
guano. As John Bellamy Foster and Brett Clark argue, such [**[a solution
in]{.underline}** [favour of the **Global North led to 'ecological
imperialism'.**]{.underline}]{.mark} Robbery continued. [Although
ecological [imperialism **shifts the rift to the peripheries** and makes
it **invisible in the centre**]{.mark}, the metabolic rift
deepens.]{.underline}37

A similar ecologically unequal exchange persists in today's globalised
capitalism. [As a solution to climate crisis, **[solar power is
essential, but]{.mark}** the [associated]{.mark} battery [technology
uses]{.mark} various kinds of [rare metals.]{.mark}]{.underline} For
example, [most of the world's [lithium is located in]{.mark} the Andean
plateau]{.underline}, so [[Chile]{.mark} has become the largest lithium
exporter.]{.underline} The Salar de Atacama salt flat is where all the
lithium of Chile is extracted. Lithium only exists [in dry

is]{.underline} thus [conducted by extracting this brine]{.underline}

[The situation is exacerbated by copper mining that]{.underline} also

[strengthening]{.mark} the [robbery-mining]{.mark} processes of lithium
and copper.]{.underline} The metabolic rift cannot be simply repaired by
new technologies. [[**Technological solutions sound attractive** because
they do not entail]{.mark} us [changing]{.mark} our current
[lifestyle]{.mark}.]{.underline} However, [for as long as the current

The third kind of metabolic shift is the temporal one. The discrepancy
between nature's time and capital's time does not immediately lead to
ecological disaster. Nature possesses 'elasticity'.40 Climate crisis is
a representative case. [Massive CO2 emission due to the excessive usage
of fossil fuels is an apparent cause of [climate change]{.mark}.
But]{.underline} its [[effects]{.mark} [do not immediately
crystallise]{.mark}, so [capital uses]{.mark} the opportunity opened up
by [the]{.mark} ensuing [time lag to make profits from previous
investment]{.mark} in drills and pipelines. **[Capital reflects the

the **[future generations.]{.mark}** The [costs]{.mark} of robbery [are
shifted]{.mark} on [to them]{.mark}.]{.underline} As a result, [future
generations suffer from what they are not responsible for.]{.underline}
Marx famously characterised such an attitude of capitalist development
with the slogan, 'Apès moi, le déluge!'

[There is huge public expectation invested in future technological
innovation against the climate crisis.]{.underline} It is true that
shifting the rift buys time for the development of new technologies.
However, [**[new technologies do not spread quickly]{.mark}** but take
years before they replace the old ones.]{.underline} Continuous temporal
shifts, [**[counting on]{.mark} future [technologies]{.mark}**[,
will]{.mark} inevitably [confront an unexpected worsening of the
crisis]{.mark}, due to positive-feedback mechanisms. [The time

**[harder to control]{.mark} the situation, [annulling]{.mark} the

5 Ecological Crisis as the Contradiction of Capitalism

The power of capital to shift the metabolic rift is astonishing. Thus,
[it is questionable whether the increase in prices]{.underline} due to
the 'end of Cheap Nature' [will lead to the 'epochal crisis' of
capitalism,]{.underline} as Jason W. Moore argues.41 Bill McKibben
better describes the historical dynamics of capitalism: '[The diminished
availability of fossil fuel is not the only limit we face.]{.underline}
In fact, [it's not even the most important. Even before we run out of
oil, we're **running out of planet'.**]{.underline}42 This is [not only
because [capital can]{.mark} [find]{.mark} **[new opportunities for a
'climate]{.mark}-change [shock doctrine']{.mark}** amidst the ecological
crisis]{.underline},43 [[but]{.mark} also because [it always]{.mark}
**[externalises]{.mark} the [negative consequences onto the Global
South.]{.mark}**]{.underline}

In this way, [[the Global South suffers from doubly negative
consequences]{.mark}. After suffering [from the robbery of]{.mark}
nature and humans under [ecological imperialism, it also faces the real
impact of ecological crisis]{.mark}]{.underline}, once [it is no longer
possible to postpone it.]{.underline} As Stephan Lessenich argues,
capital's slogan 'After us, the deluge!' becomes 'Next to us, the
deluge' (Neben uns, die Sintflut), in the age of global ecological
crisis when it is no longer possible to buy time. This is the essence of
the 'externalisation society' (Externalisierungsgesellschaft) that is
dominant in the Global North.44

[Affluent life in the Global North is obviously dependent on
robbery]{.underline} from other areas, but [this structural inequality
and injustice was long kept invisible]{.underline} through the temporal,
spatial and social shift and externalisation of the metabolic rift. This
is what Ulrich Brand and Markus Wissen call the 'imperial mode of
living' (imperiale Lebensweise). Their point is that a better life for a
certain group of people in a certain region presupposes the degradation
of living conditions for another group of people in another region.45
The imperial mode of living essentially signifies a relation of
domination and subordination. The current order of society appears
attractive and comfortable to a certain social group in the Global
North, but its real costs are imposed upon other social groups in other
areas.

Of course, [the fundamental problem is not a 'mode of living' but a
'mode of production',]{.underline} because [the tendency towards the
robbery of humans and nature and [metabolic shift is inherent to the
logic of capital.]{.mark} The imperial mode of production is constantly
reproduced and its **[violence becomes invisible]{.mark}** due to the
metabolic shift.]{.underline} Consequently, [[people]{.mark} who enjoy
the affluent life [in the **Global North are**]{.mark} first **[forced
to be 'ignorant']{.mark}**]{.underline} (Nicht-Wissen[) [**about the
structural inequality** of]{.mark} the [imperial]{.mark} mode of
[production]{.mark}, but later, as long as it promises the affluent
life, [they]{.mark} start to [accept]{.mark} it as something desirable
[and]{.mark} to [internalise it by looking away]{.mark} from the
negative consequences. They do not want to know]{.underline}
(Nicht-Wissen-Wollen). Consequently, the imperial mode of production
transforms into the 'imperial mode of living'.

However, [[it is impossible to endlessly shift the metabolic
rift.]{.mark} It is increasingly difficult to ignore the negative
consequence of metabolic rift, as the competition for robbery becomes
more brutal]{.underline} with the rapid development of China, Brazil and
India. [As the space for externalisation diminishes, the once-obscured
metabolic rift becomes increasingly visible, as the climate crisis
causes heat-waves and super-typhoons, even in the Global
North.]{.underline} Since communism is 'ultimately a question of
justice',46 climate justice is an essential component for communism.
That is why Marx's idea of communism must be radically updated in the
age of global ecological crisis.

### 1nc\-\--sustainability link 

#### \*\*Sustainability is a paradox\-\--the aff is system preservation that undermines material basis of the environment 

**Chaudhary 22** (Ajay Singh Chaudhary - Brooklyn Institute for Social
Research Doctor of Philosophy. "Sustaining What? Capitalism, Socialism,
and Climate Change." Ch. In Capitalism, Democracy, Socialism: Critical
Debates. Ed. Albena Azmanova and James Chamberlain, *Springer*,
September 2022, DOI:10.1007/978-3-031-08407-2_9 HDCSW-TM)

9.1 Sustainable Ubiquity

Anthropogenic [climate change]{.underline} has a palpable presence---in
[wildfires]{.underline}, [flooding]{.underline}, [drought]{.underline},
annual or even more frequent "once-in-a-century" [weather]{.underline}
events, dangerous levels of wet-bulb and even dry-bulb heat. Then there
is the [grave [social and economic fallout]{.mark} of these
occurrences]{.underline}. The COVID-19 pandemic itself has much to do
with climate change---with an [[intensified "gobbling" of
ecosystems]{.mark}]{.underline} that ["enables viruses to jump from
previously isolated]{.underline} wild [animals into]{.underline} the
[food system]{.underline} and agricultural laborers and then, by way of
food products and travelers, move throughout the world in a matter of
weeks."1 [Pandemics]{.underline} and other infectious disease events
[are]{.underline} already [becoming]{.underline} far more [frequent on
the]{.underline} current business-as-usual [trajectory of global
capitalism]{.underline} and anthropogenic climate change. The [[question
is no longer]{.underline}]{.mark} one of [[denial vs.
acceptance]{.underline}]{.mark} of climate science.2 [[It is about power
dynamics]{.mark} [within]{.mark}]{.underline} those [[concrete
realities]{.underline}]{.mark} in this particular conjuncture of the
Anthropocene.

Sustainability is now a commonplace term. Its connotations span from the
minimal definition of sustainability as "avoiding extinction and living
to survive and reproduce"3 to various renditions of "sustainable
development" as "development that meets the needs of the present without
compromising the ability of future generations to meet their own
needs."4 For all the attention given to
[[sustainability]{.underline}]{.mark}, it [remains a fuzzy
[concept]{.mark} that [accommodates a multitude of often conflicting
goals]{.mark}]{.underline}.5 Typically, uses of the concept involve or
gesture towards ecological concerns and relate to the broad field of
sustainable development as defined above at the Brundtland Conference in
1987. But, as previous environmental discourses of "natural"
conservation or preservation fall out of use, today's usage of the term
reflects a spreading concern with climate change.

In this chapter, I review some controversies haunting policy and
academic debates on sustainability. Irreconcilable objectives within
commitments to [[sustainability produce]{.underline}]{.mark} what I call
[[a]{.mark} "[sustainability paradox]{.mark}]{.underline}." On the one
hand, analyses, models, [[policies]{.underline}]{.mark}, and projects
[[are committed to]{.underline}]{.mark} what we might term
["[fundamental system
preservation]{.mark}"]{.underline}---[not]{.underline} in terms of
[ecological systems but]{.underline} in terms of [dominant human
socioeconomic ones]{.underline}. On the other hand, there is the
understanding that anthropogenic climate change is engendered by that
very [[socioeconomic activity]{.underline}]{.mark}. When a framework
predicated on fundamental (social) system preservation [[tries to
assimilate even minimal inputs from the climate sciences or
interdisciplinary climate research]{.underline}]{.mark}, the
[[resulting]{.mark} "[sustainable" frameworks are fundamentally
incoherent]{.mark}]{.underline}. The [sustainability
paradox]{.underline} thus [arises in seeking maximal socioecological
sustainability through safeguarding]{.underline} [the very social system
that undermines such a possibility]{.underline}.

I offer the concept of "the extractive circuit" to give us a better
guide to what Actually Existing Capitalism is today and the [[vicious
cycle capital deploys in]{.underline}]{.mark} the [[metabolic
interchange between society and nature]{.underline}]{.mark}. I then
review scientific, policy, and social scientific bodies of literature
that attempt to "solve" the sustainability paradox in terms of what
appears to be a broad social and ecological definition of
sustainability: the elaboration of a global sustainable human
"ecological niche," well-disposed to the flourishing of some 7--9
billion humans. [[Sustainability]{.underline}]{.mark}, understood in
this way, [[calls for a radical new relationship of human
society]{.mark} with its ecological niche, even as it acknowledges that
all programs for climate mitigation and adaptation are, in some sense,
an artificial human contrivance.]{.underline} Key debates, from "green
capitalism" to "degrowth" to eco-Marxism, are reviewed with regard to
the formulas they produce for achieving this objective. Finally, I
propose some broad parameters elaborating a sustainable global human
ecological niche.

9.2 Sustainable Confusion

Prior to Paul Crutzen and Eugene Stoermer's popularization of the
concept of the Anthropocene as a geologic age---one defined by human
activity as "a major geologic force" in terms of the total Earth system6
---natural science typically focused on discrete human-environmental
impacts (like those of pesticides on bird eggs and cancer cells),
whereas contemporary approaches are comprehensive and systemic.7
Prevalent natural and social scientific approaches to sustainability
tend to portray it as a balance between ecological limits, resource use,
and human needs. For example, in an interdisciplinary natural and social
scientific inquiry into ecological economics, the fundamental question
sustainability poses is: "what level of biophysical resource use is
associated with meeting people's basic needs, and can this level of
resource use be extended to all people without exceeding critical
planetary boundaries?"8 The [systemic nature of anthropogenic climate
change means]{.underline} that almost all [[contemporary analysis has
shifted into]{.mark} [thinking beyond knock-on effects and is instead
concerned with how social and natural systems are
co-imbricated]{.mark}]{.underline}. [Seemingly distant social
phenomena]{.underline} can [have ecological implications]{.underline},
and there is nothing particularly more "natural" about Holocene
conditions than Anthropocene ones. And yet, as scholars like Andreas
Malm have argued adamantly, the case can be overstated---humans do not
produce all of nature.9

Obviously, the [sustainability of ecosystem health is hardly the same
as]{.underline} the [concern for sustainability in sustainable
development studies, even if]{.underline} some of [the latter
incorporate ecological concerns]{.underline} and some of the former,
socioeconomic analysis. [Sustainable development]{.underline} in policy
is usually the assumed goal.10 But not only [is]{.underline}
sustainability in such studies [ill-defined]{.underline}, it is
[often]{.underline} here [at odds with itself]{.underline}. Like many
mainstream policy approaches, [[sustainability policy is focused
fundamentally on understandings of ecology and economy that take as
given]{.mark}]{.underline} the [continuity and maintenance of [current
global socioeconomic systems]{.mark}]{.underline}. [[These are
incommensurate goals prima facie]{.mark};]{.underline} [[the
sustainability paradox leaks into]{.underline}]{.mark} even many
[[deliberate attempts to resolve it]{.mark}.]{.underline} Even in the
relatively nascent interdisciplinary field of "sustainability science"
[the core question---"What is to be sustained?"---fails to be
addressed]{.underline}.11

The [[Paris Accords are a key example]{.underline}]{.mark} of how this
question gets avoided, thereby bringing to light the sustainability
paradox. Although the Accords are treated with reverence in many media,
policy, and political circles, even at their inception in 2015, assuming
universal compliance to the non-binding treaty, [it was technically
already impossible to reach]{.underline} the [stated targets of keeping
global temperatures to a less than 1.5 °C]{.underline} increase above
pre-industrial levels over the next 80 years, [within the treaty's
framework.]{.underline} In the simplest terms, [even with a reduction in
the rate of carbon emissions, emissions in toto are on track to increase
by about 3% per year.]{.underline} To hold to the goals of the climate
treaty, [actual emissions---not their rate]{.underline}---[would have to
decrease]{.underline} in the range of [2--3% per year]{.underline}.
Given that [this is impossible under current socioeconomic
conditions]{.underline}, [in most likely scenarios, the 2 °C
increase]{.underline} threshold [will be passed by 2050]{.underline},
[perhaps]{.underline} [as early as the mid-2030s]{.underline}.12
Δ1.5--[2 °]{.underline}C is [for the vast majority of people on the
planet]{.underline}---although not everyone, universally, together, as I
have shown elsewhere13---[already a catastrophic situation]{.underline}.
This is true even for affluent majorities in the developed geographies,
as evidenced by assessment on a host of standard measures, [including
insurmountable prospects for]{.underline} many [coastal
regions]{.underline}, further [food system destabilization]{.underline},
and the direct effects of "[dangerous heat," rampant disease, poverty,
etc.]{.underline} The recent report from the Intergovernmental Panel on
Climate Change (IPCC) on a Δ1.5 °C world paints an even bleaker picture
for a Δ2 °C world. [This is all without considering]{.underline} further
[social factors aggravating the situation, such as resource warfare and
mass migration]{.underline}.14

9.3 What We Talk About When We Talk About Sustainability

[[Common measures of climate change]{.underline}]{.mark} do not track
population growth per se; they do not track increasing consumption from
developing states; they do not track individual consumption choices.
They seem unaffected by common climate interventions, from fuel
efficiency standards to the Paris Accords. They [[track economic
growth]{.underline}]{.mark}.15 [This link is well-established and
uncontroversial]{.underline} (cited for example at the IPCC's highest
confidence levels of "robust evidence and high agreement")16---[[this is
why so much debate surrounds questions of absolute
decoupling]{.underline}]{.mark}, i.e. disconnecting economic growth from
greenhouse gas emissions and other climate metrics. Whether one is
discussing the industrial revolution in the nineteenth century or "the
Great Acceleration"---the period beginning in the mid-twentieth century
of neargeometric, interlinked socioeconomic expansion and anthropogenic
transformation of Earth systems---growth is better understood as
increased economic activity in pursuit of proftability.17 Growth also
must be addressed in more abstract terms, but the centrality of
capitalism to these developments is also uncontroversial. As the
economist Nicholas Stern once claimed, climate change is capitalism's
"largest ever market failure." The implication of Stern's argument is
that climate change is an example of the market malfunctioning---with
just the right policy fix, capitalism should provide its own solution.
In 2012, the geophysicist Brad Werner proffered a rather different
thesis in a talk colorfully entitled "Is the Earth F\*\*ked?" As
Christopher Wright and Daniel Nyberg helpfully summarize: ["we are in
this mess not because the market system is not working well enough but
because it is working too well."]{.underline}18

Werner is far from alone in conclusions like this. Closer to the
generally circumspect norm in the natural sciences concerning social and
political analyses, one finds arguments like those in the now famous
so-called "Hothouse Earth" paper for PNAS ("Trajectories of the Earth
System in the Anthropocene"). Leading a team of climate scientists, Will
Steffen, Johan Rockström, and Katherine Richardson, argue:

> The [present dominant socioeconomic system]{.underline}, however, [is
> based on high-carbon economic growth and exploitative resource
> use]{.underline}. [Attempts to modify this system have met with some
> success locally but little success globally in reducing greenhouse gas
> emissions or building]{.underline} more [effective stewardship of the
> biosphere.]{.underline} [Incremental linear changes to the present
> socioeconomic system are not enough to stabilize the Earth
> System]{.underline}. [[Widespread, rapid, and fundamental
> transformations will]{.underline}]{.mark} likely [[be required to
> reduce]{.mark}]{.underline} the [[risk of crossing the threshold and
> locking in the Hothouse Earth]{.underline}]{.mark} pathway...19

Overall, the paper's concern is with how much uncertainty there is in
climate models and how often this uncertainty is cast in favor of overly
optimistic outcomes. Echoing many other climate researchers, Steffen et
al collect evidence that [climate change does not happen linearly,
but]{.underline} can instead proceed [through thresholds, triggers,
tipping points, and cascading effects]{.underline}. What is easy to miss
in the paper is the subtle socioeconomic argument. The rhetoric is
understated, but the argument is charged:
[[capitalism]{.underline}]{.mark}---that is, "the present dominant
socioeconomic system"---[[is fundamentally dependent on "high-carbon
economic growth and exploitative resource use]{.mark}."]{.underline}
Overall, [the case simply reflects the empirical record]{.underline}.
Similar to the IPCC calls for "unprecedented transformation," the paper
suggests widespread, rapid, and fundamental transformations to ensure a
best-case scenario for the vast majority of people on Earth. This can be
achieved with already existing technology, but if you look at the
appendices Steffen et al include, they spell out conditions that cannot
be reconciled with existing Global North economies. Furthermore, they
are straightforward about [even the most modest techno-fixes such
as]{.underline} Carbon Capture and Storage ([[CSS]{.underline}]{.mark}),
which they correctly note [are [still largely theoretical]{.mark} and at
best energy-intensive to produce.]{.underline}20 "[Incremental linear
changes are not enough,"]{.underline} the authors note. [Small-bore
reforms and modest adjustments to the "dominant socioeconomic system"
are simply not credible forms of climate mitigation and
adaptation]{.underline}.
